Liability for Obstructions in Public and Private Paths
Ruling on diyah for deaths caused by road obstructions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for death or property damage caused by obstructions in public and private paths, and distinguish between cases with and without the Imam's permission.

Core Idea
A road obstruction causing death triggers diyah on the aqila, but only if the Imam did not permit it.

This passage from the Book of Diyat (Blood-Money) addresses a common scenario: someone places a stone or digs a well in a road, and a person or animal dies as a result. The ruling distinguishes between public and private paths, and between human and animal victims. It also introduces a key exception: if the Imam (ruler) permitted the obstruction, there is no liability. Understanding these distinctions is essential for applying the Hanafi principles of causal liability (ḍamān) and the role of the ʿāqila (family group) in paying diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Determine whether the obstruction occurred in a public thoroughfare (ṭarīq al-ʿāmma) or a private path (ṭarīq ghayr nāfidh). The legal treatment differs based on the type of path and whether permission was obtained.
In a private path, even harmless obstruction requires consent of all partners.
2
Determine liability for human death
If a human dies as a direct result of falling onto an obstruction (e.g., a stone or well) in a public road, the diyah is wajib upon the ʿāqila of the person who created the obstruction, because he is a cause (mutasabbib) through transgression (taʿaddī).
Diyah for homicide is borne by the ʿāqila, not the individual's own wealth.
3
Determine liability for animal death
If an animal (bahīma) is killed by the same obstruction, the liability is on the individual who created the obstruction, not the ʿāqila. This is because the ʿāqila only covers human life, not property.
The individual pays the value of the animal from his own wealth.
4
Note the condition of Imam's permission
Liability for both human and animal cases exists only if the Imam (ruler) did not permit the obstruction. If the Imam permitted it, there is no liability because the actor is not considered a transgressor (ghayr mutaʿaddī) since he acted on the order of one with authority over public rights.
The Imam's permission is a complete defense. The text implies that an authorized act is not a transgression.
5
Analyze the special case of death from internal cause
Abū Ḥanīfa holds that if a person falls into a well but dies from hunger or suffocation (not from the fall itself), the digger is not liable. The death is attributed to an internal cause (maʿnan fī nafsihi), not the fall.
This ruling shows that causation must be direct; mere presence of the obstruction is not sufficient if the death is independently caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات / أنواعها وأحكامها
وفي غير نافذ لا يسعه بلا إذنِ الشركاء، وإن لم يضر.
بالناس لا يجوز له أن يفعل، وإن لم يُضِرَّ بهم يجوز، لكن مع ذلك يكون لكل واحد نقضه؛ لأنَّه تصرُّف في الحقِّ المشترك، فلكل نقضه كما في الملكِ المشترك مع أنه لم يضر، وفي غير نافذ لا يسعه بلا إذنِ الشركاء، وإن لم يضر. وضَمِنَ عاقلتُهُ دَيَّةَ مَن مات بسقوطها، كما لو وضع حجراً، أو حفر بئراً في الطريق، فتلف به نفس، فإن تَلِفَ به بهيمةٌ ضَمِنَ هو إن لم يأذن به الإمام، فإن الضمانَ في جميع ما ذُكِرَ بإحداث شيءٍ في طريق العامة إنما يكون إذا لم يأذن به الإمام، فإن أذن أو مات واقع في بئر طريق جوعاً، أو غماً فلا، هذا عند. قوله: وفي غير نافذ.... الخ؛ أي من أحدث شيئاً من هذه المذكورات في طريق طائفة مخصوصة لا يجوز له ذلك؛ لأنها مملوكة لأهلها فلا يجوز التصرف فيها إلا باذن الكل. قوله: وضمن عاقلته دِيَة من مات.... الخ؛ أي إذا أحدث شيئاً من هذه المذكورات فسقط على إنسان فهلك فضمنَ الدِّية على العاقلة؛ لأنه مسبب لتلفه، متعدّ بشغله هواء الطريق، وهذا من أسباب الضمان وهو الأصل في هذا الباب. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: ضمن هو؛ أي الواضع أو الحافر؛ لأنه متعد، فيضمن، يتولّد من تعديه، غير أن العاقلة تتحمّل النفس دون المال، فكان ضمان البهيمة في ماله، وإلقاء التراب واتحاد الطين في الطريق بمنزلة إلقاء الحجر والخشبة لما ذكرنا من أنه متعد فيه. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: فإن أذن...الخ؛ أي فإن أذن الإمام فلا يضمن؛ لأنه غير متعد، حيث فعل ما فعل بأمر من له الولاية في حقوق العامة. قوله: فلا؛ أي فلا يضمن، قال في «الهداية»: لا ضمان على الحافر عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه مات لمعنى في نفسه والضمان إنّما يجب إذا مات من الوقوع.
The Book of Diyat: Types and Rulings. And in a non-through path, he cannot do it without the permission of the partners, even if he does not harm. People: it is not permissible for him to act, and if he does not harm them it is permissible, but nevertheless each one has the right to annul it, because it is a disposition in a joint right, so each has the right to annul it as in joint ownership even though he did not harm. And in a non-through path, he cannot do it without the permission of the partners, even if he does not harm. And his aqila pays the diyah of one who dies from its falling, as if he placed a stone or dug a well in the road, and a person perishes by it, then if an animal perishes by it, he guarantees it if the Imam did not permit. For the liability in all that was mentioned regarding creating something in a public road occurs only if the Imam did not permit; if he permits, or one dies falling into a road well from hunger or suffocation, then no. This is according to... His statement: 'And in a non-through path...' etc.; meaning whoever creates something from these mentioned things in a path of a specific group, it is not permissible for him to do so, because it is owned by its people, so it is not permissible to dispose of it except with the permission of all. His statement: 'And his aqila pays the diyah of one who dies...' etc.; meaning if he creates something from these mentioned things and it falls on a person and he perishes, then the diyah is paid by the aqila, because he is a cause of his death, transgressing by occupying the air of the road, and this is among the causes of liability and it is the basis of this chapter. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah'. His statement: 'He guarantees it'; i.e., the placer or digger, because he is a transgressor, so he guarantees, from his transgression, but the aqila bears the homicide and not the wealth, so the guarantee of the animal is upon his wealth, and throwing dirt and mixing mud in the road is like throwing a stone or wood, for what we mentioned that it is transgression in it. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah'. His statement: 'If he permits...' etc.; meaning if the Imam permits, he does not guarantee, because he is not a transgressor, since he acted by the command of one who has authority over public rights. His statement: 'Then no'; i.e., he does not guarantee. He said in 'Al-Hidayah': There is no guarantee on the digger according to Abu Hanifa, because he died from something internal to himself, and guarantee is only obligatory if he dies from the fall.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Diyat deals with the ruling on liability for causing death or injury by placing obstructions in pathways. It distinguishes between a path belonging to a specific group (private) and a public thoroughfare. For a private path, one cannot place any obstruction without the permission of all partners; even if it causes no harm, each partner has the right to annul the act. For a public road, if one places a stone or digs a well and a human dies as a result, the diyah is paid by the aqila (the extended paternal family) because the person is a mubashir (direct cause) or mutasabbib (indirect cause) through transgression in occupying the airspace of the road. However, if an animal is killed, the individual pays the value from his own wealth, as the aqila does not bear liability for property. The key condition for liability is that the Imam (the ruler) did not permit the act; if the Imam permits, there is no liability. The passage also mentions a specific opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa: if someone dies in a well not from the fall but from hunger or suffocation, the digger is not liable, as the death is attributed to an internal cause rather than the act of falling. The commentator supports these rulings with references to Al-Hidayah, a central Hanafi text.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The extended paternal family (tribe or close relatives) who bear the responsibility of paying diyah for unintentional homicide on behalf of the perpetrator.
دية (diya): The monetary compensation paid by the aqila (or the offender) for causing death or injury, typically applicable to unintentional homicide.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to pay compensation for harm caused to another's life or property due to transgression (ta'ddi).
تعدي (taʿaddī): Transgression or overstepping legal bounds; a key element for liability in Hanafi law of torts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person places a stone in a public road and someone dies as a result, who pays the diyah?
A) The individual who placed the stone
B) The aqila of that individual
C) The Imam (ruler)
D) No one, because it was unintentional

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for death caused by a road obstruction falls on the aqila, and for property on the individual, with the Imam's permission being a complete defense.

Liability for Removing an Obstruction: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for stone removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on liability when a person removes a stone placed by another, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each view.

Core Idea
A later deliberate act can sever the causal chain and transfer liability, but the Sahibayn differ on the scope.

In Hanafi fiqh, causation is key to liability. A seemingly simple act—removing a stone from a road—raises a deep question: does the remover assume liability for harms that result from that stone? The three great Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—each offer a distinct analysis based on their understanding of causal chains and the permissibility of the actions involved. This lesson walks through their reasoning and the practical differences that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage and describe the factual situation: person A places a stone in the road, person B removes it, and person C is injured by that stone. What are the possible chains of causation?
The stone is the instrument of harm, but multiple acts contribute to the accident.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds person B (the remover) liable. His key concept is 'infaskh' (dissolution): the first person's act is legally dissolved by the second's deliberate removal. The second becomes the sole cause.
Analogies: carrying goods that fall on someone; entering a mosque not of one's neighborhood with items.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his distinction
Abu Yusuf distinguishes by cause of death: if the victim dies of hunger (because unable to reach food after falling), the remover is liable; if of suffocation (from the fall itself), the original digger (if a well) is liable. Suffocation has no other cause than the fall, whereas hunger could have been alleviated if food were near.
This shows a finer analysis of causality based on specificity.
4
State Muhammad's position and his reasoning
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds the remover liable in all cases: the fall is the cause of both suffocation and hunger, because the fall isolates the victim from food. Thus the remover's act is the sole legal cause.
He rejects the distinction; the fall is the common link.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mosque analogy
The practical difference: in a suffocation case, Abu Yusuf would hold the original digger liable, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad would hold the remover. The passage extends to entering a mosque: Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on whether the mosque belongs to one's own neighborhood; the Sahibayn hold that acts of worship are not conditional on safety.
The mosque analogy reveals a deeper principle: permissibility (ibaha) vs. worship (qurb) in determining liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن نحى حجرا وضعَهُ آخر فعطب به رجلٌ ضَمِن؛ لأنَّ فعلَ الأَوَّلِ انفسخ بفعل الثاني، فالضمانُ على الثَّاني، كمَن حمل شيئاً في الطّريقِ فسقط على آخر، أو دخل بحصير، أو قنديل، أو حصاة في مسجد غيره. وقال أبو يوسف: إن مات جوعاً فكذلك، وإن مات غماً فالحافر ضامن له؛ لأنه لا سبب للغـم سـوى الوقوع، أما الجوع لا يختص بالبئر، وقال محمد: هو ضامن في الوجوه كلها؛ لأنه إنّما حدث بسبب الوقوع إذ لولاه لكان الطعام قريباً منه. قوله: ومَن نحى... الخ؛ نحى بالنون والحاء المهملة المشدّدة؛ أي أبعد عن الطريق الحجر الذي وضعه غيره. كذا في الجلبي. قوله: كمن حمل شيئاً في الطريق فسقط منه على آخر؛ أي على شخص آخر، ضَمِنَ الحامل سواء تلف بالوقوع أو بالعشرة به بعد الوقوع؛ لأنّ حمل المتاع في الطريق على رأسه أو ظهره مباح له، لكنّه مقيد بشرط السلامة بمنزلة الرمي إلى الهدف والصيد. قوله: أو دخل بحصير... الخ؛ أي يضمن بهذه الأفعال إن كانت في مسجدِ غيره، وإن كانت في مسجدِ حيّه لا يضمن قالوا: هذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا: لا يضمن في الوجهين؛ لأنّ هذه الأفعال من القرب، وكلّ أحدٍ مأذون في إقامتها، فلا يتقيَّدُ لشرط السلامة، كما إذا فعله من هو في غير حيه بإذن واحد من أهل المسجد. لأبي حنيفة له، وهو الفرق: إنّ التدبير فيما يتعلّق بالمسجد لأهله دون غيره؛ غير كَنصب الإمام، واختيار المتولى، وفتح بابه وإغلاقه، فكان فعلهم مباحاً مطلقاً مقيد بشرط السلامة، وفعل غيرهم تعدّياً أو مباحاً مقيّداً به، وقصد القربة لا ينافي الغرامة إذا أخطأ الطريق.
And whoever removes a stone placed by another, and a man is injured by it, he is liable, because the act of the first is dissolved by the act of the second, so liability is on the second, like one who carries something in the road and it falls on another, or enters with a mat, or a lantern, or a pebble into a masjid of someone else. And Abu Yusuf said: If he died of hunger, likewise (i.e., the second is liable), but if he died of suffocation, then the digger (the first) is liable for him, because there is no cause for suffocation except the fall; as for hunger, it is not specific to the well. And Muhammad said: He (the second) is liable in all cases, because it only occurred due to the fall, for if not for it, food would have been near him. His statement: 'And whoever removes...' etc.; 'naha' with noon and unpointed ha with shaddah, meaning he moved away from the road the stone placed by another. Thus in al-Jalabi. His statement: 'Like one who carries something in the road and it falls on another'; i.e., on another person, the carrier is liable whether he perishes by the fall or by contact after the fall, because carrying goods on his head or back is permissible for him, but it is conditional on safety, like shooting at a target or hunting. His statement: 'Or enters with a mat...' etc.; i.e., he is liable for these acts if they are in a masjid of someone else; if they are in the masjid of his own neighborhood, he is not liable. They said: This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not liable in both cases, because these acts are acts of worship, and everyone is permitted to perform them, so they are not conditional on safety, just as if one who is not in his neighborhood does it with permission of one of the people of the masjid. For Abu Hanifa there is a distinction: the administration of what pertains to the masjid belongs to its people, not to others, such as appointing the imam, choosing the trustee, opening and closing its door. So their act is absolutely permissible but conditional on safety, whereas the act of others is either transgression or permissible but conditional on safety. The intention of gaining reward does not negate the indemnity if he misses the mark.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the specific case where a person removes a stone that another had placed in the road, and a third person trips on it and dies or is injured. According to Abu Hanifa, the second person (who removed the stone) is liable, because the first person's act (placing the stone) is effectively dissolved by the removal. The liability is transferred to the second person, analogous to someone carrying an object in the road that falls on another; the carrier is liable even if the object falls after the carrier puts it down, because the act of carrying is conditionally permitted subject to safety. Abu Yusuf distinguishes the cause of death: if the victim dies of hunger (because he fell into the well and could not get food), the remover is liable; but if he dies from suffocation (the well's air), then the original digger is liable, because suffocation is caused only by the fall itself. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds the second person liable in all cases, because the victim's inability to access food is a consequence of the fall caused by the second person's act. The passage also extends the analogy to entering a mosque with items (mat, lantern, pebble) – Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on whether the mosque belongs to one's own neighborhood or someone else's; if another's, the person is liable because he doesn't have full authority over the mosque. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that since entering a mosque for prayer is an act of worship permitted to everyone, it is not conditional on safety, so no liability. The commentary clarifies that according to Abu Hanifa, the management of the mosque belongs to its people (local community), while others are either transgressors or conditionally permitted, and the intention of worship does not exempt from liability if harm occurs.

Key Terms
انفسخ (infaskha): The act of the first person (placing the stone) is legally dissolved or nullified by the act of the second (removing it), transferring liability.
مقيد بشرط السلامة (muqayyad bi-sharṭ al-salāma): An action that is normally permissible but becomes conditional upon not causing harm; if harm occurs, liability arises because the action was not absolutely unqualified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if person A places a stone in the road, person B removes it, and person C trips on that stone and dies, who is liable for diyah?
A) Person A
B) Person B
C) Both A and B equally
D) Neither, because it was an accident

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a deliberate later act (like removing an obstruction) can dissolve the causal link of the original actor, transferring liability to the remover; however, the Sahibayn offer nuanced refinements based on the specific cause of harm.

Liability for Harm Caused by a Person Sitting in a Mosque
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability of a sitter in mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the liability of a sitter in a mosque and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given fact pattern.

Core Idea
Prayer protects from liability in a mosque; mere sitting does not—yet later fatwa grants blanket immunity.

This passage examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about liability when a person sitting in a mosque inadvertently causes harm to another. Does the sitter's state—praying or not—affect his liability? Abu Hanifa draws a sharp line, but his two disciples disagree entirely. We will trace each side's proof, analyze the distinction between acts of worship and permissible acts, and see which view ultimately prevailed for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person sits in a mosque, and due to his presence, harm occurs (e.g., a mat falls, a lamp drops, or a blind person trips over him). Determine whether the sitter is liable (daman) for compensation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, if the sitter is praying, he is not liable because prayer is a qurbah (act of worship) not restricted to safety. If he is not praying, his sitting is mubah (permissible) and therefore conditioned on safety; thus he is liable.
This also applies to introducing objects: no liability in one's own mosque, but liability in another's mosque.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that no liability attaches to a sitter in a mosque, whether praying or not, and whether objects are introduced into any mosque, because qurbah (e.g., sitting for prayer) is not restricted to safety, and even non-prayer sitting is mubah but not automatically restricted.
They reject Abu Hanifa's distinction based on mosque ownership.
4
Understand the dalil for each
Abu Hanifa's proof: Management of a mosque belongs to its residents; a non-resident's act is mubah and thus restricted by safety. The Sahibayn's proof: Qurbah is unrestricted; sitting in a mosque, even without prayer, is mubah but not inherently restricted because the mosque is a place of worship for all.
Also, Muhammad adds a nuance for worn objects: customary clothing is like mubah, but unusual attire (like a dervish's sack) is treated as a carried object, imposing liability even if praying.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, a non-praying sitter who causes harm must compensate; under the Sahibayn, he does not. Later scholars (al-Halwani) preferred the Sahibayn's view, and the fatwa is on their position.
Thus, in a fatwa today, no liability for a sitter in a mosque regardless of prayer status or mosque ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أو جلس فيه غير مصلّ، فعطب به أحد لا مَن سقط منه رداءٌ لَيسَه ، أو أدخل هذه الأشياء في مسجد حيه أو جلس فيه مصليّاً

أو جلس فيه غير مصل، فعطب به أحد، نحو إن سقط الحصير أو القنديل على أحد، أو سقط الظرف الذي فيه الحصاة على أحد، أو كان جالساً غير مصلّ فسقط عليه أعمى بضَمِن. لا من سقط منه رداء ليسه، أو أدخل هذه الأشياء في مسجد حيه أو جلس فيه مصليّاً، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: لا يضمن بإدخال هذه الأشياء في المسجد سواء كان مسجد حيّه أو غير مسجد حيه؛ لأن القربة لا تتقيد بشرط السلامة. له: أن تدبير المسجد لأهله دون غيرهم، ففعل الغير مباح فيكون مقيداً بشرط السلامة. وعندهما: الجالس في المسجد لا يضمن، سواء جلس للصلاة أو غير الصلاة. فالحاصل أن الجالس للصلاة في المسجد لا يضمن عند أبي حنيفة سواء في مسجد حيه أو غيره، والجالس لغير الصلاة يضمن سواء في مسجد حيه أو غيره. وفي سقوط الرداء إنما لا يضمن عند محمد إذا لبس ما يلبس عادة، أما إن لبس ما لا يلبس عادة كجوالق القلندرين، فسقط على إنسان فهلك يضمن، فهذا اللبس بمنزلة الحمل، وفي الحمل يضمن. وعن محمد: إنه إذا لبس ما لا يلبس عادة، فهو كالحامل؛ لأن الحاجة لا تدعو إلى لبسه عادة، فتضمن كما قال الشارح بقوله: وعند محمد... الخ.

أما قوله: لا من سقط منه رداء... الخ، أي لا يضمن، والفرق بين المحمول والملبوس أن حامل الشيء قاصد لحفظه، فلا حرج في التقييد بوصف السلامة، واللابس لا يقصد حفظ ما يلبسه، ففي التقيد بما ذكرنا حرج، فجعلناه مباحاً مطلقاً.

وهذا كله عند أبي حنيفة. وأما عندهما: لا يضمن مطلقاً، سواء كان في الصلاة أو لا.

(وأما ما ذكر من الشهادة على الزنا) فإن شهادته من حيث أنها شهادة في حقوق الله تعالى قربة، ولكن بنقصان عدد شهادة الزنا انقلبت قذفاً، فيجب حد القذف على الشاهد، والطريق فيما نحن الاستئذان من أهله وعبارات المتون تدل على أن الفتوى على قول الإمام، وفي تكملة البحر وشرح الزيلعي: قال الحلواني: أكثر المشايخ أخذوا بقولهما، وعليه الفتوى، والله أعلم.

الأشياء في المسجد سواء كان مسجد حيّه أو غير مسجد حيه؛ لأن القربة لا تتقيد بشرط السلامة. له: أن تدبير المسجد لأهله دون غيرهم، ففعل الغير مباح فيكون مقيداً بشرط السلامة. وعندهما: الجالس في المسجد لا يضمن، سواء جلس للصلاة أو غير الصلاة. فالحاصل أن الجالس للصلاة في المسجد لا يضمن عند أبي حنيفة سواء في مسجد حيه أو غيره، والجالس لغير الصلاة يضمن سواء في مسجد حيه أو غيره. وفي سقوط الرداء إنما لا يضمن عند محمد الله إذا لبس ما يلبس عادة، أما إن لبس ما لا يلبس عادة كجوالق القلندرين، فسقط على إنسان فهلك يضمن، فهذا اللبس بمنزلة الحمل، وفي الحمل يضمن. وعن محمد الله: إنه إذا لبس ما لا يلبس عادة، فهو كالحامل؛ لأن الحاجة لا تدعو إلى لبسه عادة، فتضمن كما قال الشارح الله بقوله: وعند محمد... الخ.
Book of Blood-Money / Its Types and Rulings. 'Or if someone other than a praying person sat in the mosque, and someone was injured by him, except one from whom a garment fell that he was wearing, or he introduced these objects into the mosque of his neighborhood or sat there while praying.' Then the commentary: 'Or if one who is not praying sat there, and someone was injured by him, such as if a mat or a lamp fell on someone, or a vessel containing pebbles fell on someone, or if he was sitting not praying and a blind man fell on him, he is liable. Except one from whom a garment fell that he was wearing, or he introduced these objects into the mosque of his neighborhood or sat there while praying. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): He is not liable for introducing these objects into the mosque, whether it is the mosque of his neighborhood or another, because an act of worship (qurbah) is not restricted by the condition of safety. His (Abu Hanifa's) argument: The management of the mosque belongs to its residents, not others, so the action of a non-resident is merely permissible (mubah) and thus is restricted by the condition of safety. According to the two: The one sitting in the mosque is not liable, whether he sits for prayer or otherwise. So the conclusion: The one sitting for prayer in the mosque is not liable according to Abu Hanifa, whether in his neighborhood mosque or another. And the one sitting for other than prayer is liable, whether in his neighborhood mosque or another. Regarding the falling of a garment: He is not liable according to Muhammad if he wore what is customarily worn. But if he wore what is not customarily worn, like a sack of dervishes, and it fell on a person and killed him, he is liable, for this wearing is like carrying, and in carrying he is liable. And from Muhammad: If he wore what is not customarily worn, he is like a carrier, because there is no customary need to wear it, so he is liable, as the commentator said: 'And according to Muhammad... etc.' As for his saying: 'Except one from whom a garment fell...', i.e., he is not liable. The difference between a carried object and worn clothing is that the carrier intends to preserve the object, so there is no difficulty in conditioning safety; but the wearer does not intend to preserve what he wears, so conditioning safety would be restrictive, so we consider it absolutely permissible. This is all according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two: He is not liable absolutely, whether in prayer or otherwise. (As for what was mentioned about bearing witness to zina): his testimony, insofar as it is testimony concerning the rights of Allah, is an act of worship, but due to the deficiency in the number of witnesses for zina, it becomes slander, so the hadd penalty for slander is obligatory upon the witness. The way here is to seek permission from the people (of that mosque). And the texts indicate that the fatwa is on the statement of the Imam. And in Takmilat al-Bahr and Sharh al-Zayla'i: Al-Halwani said: Most of the later scholars took the position of the two, and the fatwa is on that. And Allah knows best. (Then a repeated summary on page 516.)
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability (daman) when a person sitting in a mosque causes harm to another, such as by a mat or lamp falling, or by a blind person tripping over him. The core issue is whether the sitter's state (praying or not) and the type of object (carried vs. worn) affect liability. Abu Hanifa holds that a person sitting in prayer is engaged in a qurbah (act of worship) and therefore is not liable for harm caused directly by his sitting; the act of prayer is intrinsically beneficial and not restricted to safety conditions. In contrast, a person sitting without prayer is engaged in a mubah (permissible) act, which is conditioned on safety, so he is liable if harm results. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that qurbah is not restricted to safety and that sitting in the mosque, whether for prayer or not, is a mubah act; hence, no liability attaches to the sitter himself. The text further notes a nuance from Muhammad regarding worn objects: if a person wears a garment that is customary (like a cloak) and it falls, he is not liable; but if he wears something unusual (like a dervish's sack) and it falls and kills, he is liable, likening it to carrying an object. Finally, the passage reports that later scholars (al-Halwani) preferred the Sahibayn's view, and the fatwa is based on their position. The example of testimony for zina is given to illustrate that a qurbah can become a sin if conditions are violated.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation (blood-money or damages) for harm caused.
قربة (qurbah): An act of worship that brings one closer to Allah, such as prayer.
مباح (mubah): A permissible act, neither commanded nor prohibited, which may be conditioned on safety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) in the Hanafi school, is a person sitting in a mosque without praying liable for compensation if a mat falls and injures someone?
A) Yes, because he is not praying.
B) No, because no liability attaches to any sitter in a mosque.
C) Yes, but only if the mat belongs to him.
D) No, but only if he is in his own mosque.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative ruling is that a person sitting in a mosque is not liable for accidental harm caused to another, regardless of whether he is praying or not.

Liability of 'Aqila in Shared Wall Collapse: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'aqila liability fractions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on 'aqila liability when a shared wall is demolished, explain the underlying 'illa (cause) dispute, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Whether the wall's collapse is attributed to a single cause or multiple portions determines the 'aqila's share.

In this passage, the Hanafi school examines the liability of the 'aqila when one owner requests demolition of a shared wall. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the fraction of diyah the 'aqila must pay. Their disagreement stems from a deeper usul question: is the cause of death (thiqal al-ha'it) one and indivisible, or divisible by ownership portions? Let's unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the question
Identify the case: a wall shared by five owners, one requests demolition, the wall collapses and kills a man. Who is liable for blood money?
The request is the key action triggering liability.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa’s position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa says the 'aqila pays 1/5 of diyah. His dalil: the request is valid only for the requester's one-fifth share; the remaining collapse occurs without request. Also, the cause (weight/depth) is single, so liability is divided by ownership shares.
The 'illa is one: the total weight/depth of the wall/well.
3
Identify the Sahibayn’s position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the 'aqila pays 1/2. Their dalil: the wall can be divided into two halves—the requester's portion and the others' portion. Each portion is an independent cause, like multiple separate injuries (e.g., lion wound or snake bite).
They see the cause as divisible by portions.
4
Analyze the key disagreement: nature of the ‘illa
Abu Hanifa: the predetermined weight (thiqal muqaddar) is a single cause applying to the whole structure; therefore death is attributed to that one cause and divided proportionally. Sahibayn: each owner's portion has its own weight; the collapse in the requester's portion is a distinct cause from the rest, leading to half liability.
This disagreement reflects a broader usul principle: how to identify the 'illa.
5
Determine the practical difference and usul principle
The thamarah: in a five-share wall, Abu Hanifa gives 1/5, Sahibayn give 1/2. This also affects analogous cases like digging a well or building a wall. The usul principle is the definition of the 'illa: single vs. multiple.
The text does not specify the mufta bihi; both views are recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حائط بين خمسة طلب نقضُهُ من أحدهم، وسقط على رجلٍ ضَمِنَ العاقلة خمس الدية، كما ضمنُوا ثلثيها إن حفر أحد ثلاثة في دارهم بئراً، أو بنى حائطاً (حائط بين خمسة طلب نقضه من أحدهم، وسقط على رجلٍ ضَمِنَ العاقلة خمس الدية، كما ضمنُوا ثلثيها إن حفر أحد ثلاثة في دارهم بئراً، أو بنى حائطاً) : أي ضمن عاقلةُ مَن طلب منه النقض خمس الدية ؛ لأنَّ الطلب صح في الخمس، وضَمِنَ عاقلة حافر البئر، وباني الحائط ثُلُثَي الدِّيَة ؛ لأنَّ الحافر والباني في الثلثين متعد.

(۱) تكملة البحر» (٤٠٤:٨). (۲) (العناية» (١٠ : ٣٢٤). (۳) ((الهداية» (٤ : ١٩٧).
A wall between five people, one of them requested its demolition, and it fell on a man. The 'aqila guarantees a fifth of the blood money, just as they guarantee two-thirds if one of three dug a well in their house or built a wall. (Meaning: the 'aqila of the one who requested the demolition guarantees a fifth of the blood money because the request is valid for a fifth; and the 'aqila of the well-digger and wall-builder guarantee two-thirds of the blood money because the digger and builder are aggressors in the two-thirds.) [2] This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: they guarantee half in the wall, digging, and building. As for the wall: because the destruction is considered in the portion of the one from whom it was requested, not in the portion of others, so it is divided into two parts, like in the wounding of a lion or the bite of a snake, or a human wound. In the case of digging and building, the destruction in the portion of the owner does not cause liability, and in the portion of the usurper it does, so it is divided into two parts. (If the wind throws it, he is liable. This is not like a stone placed on the road by someone, and the wind moves it from place to place, and a man trips over it; he is not liable. So in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'.) [11] His statement: 'Guarantees the 'aqila a fifth of the blood money' – Do you not see that if he had a witness for all of them, then the wall fell on a man, each one would owe a fifth of the blood money, so he left witnessing. For the rest, the obligation on the one who had a witness against him does not increase. So in 'al-'Inaya' (2). [2] His statement: 'This is according to Abu Hanifa' – He has the argument that death occurred from a single cause: the predetermined weight in the wall and the predetermined depth in the well. Because the fundamental weight and depth are not causes; it is slight until each part is considered a cause, so the causes combine. Once it is established that the cause is one, death is attributed to that single cause, then divided among its owners according to ownership, contrary to wounds, where each wound is itself a cause of destruction, small or large as known, except when there is competition, it is attributed to all due to lack of priority. So in 'al-Hidaya' (3).
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of the 'aqila when a shared wall collapses due to one owner's request for demolition. The ruling is that the 'aqila of the requesting owner guarantees one-fifth of the blood money (diyah), while if three owners dig a well or build a wall, the 'aqila guarantees two-thirds. Abu Hanifa holds that the request is valid only for the portion of the requesting owner, hence one-fifth. The two (Sahibayn) argue that the wall is divided into two halves: the requesting owner's portion and the rest, so liability is half. They analogize to cases of a wound from a lion or snake bite where each portion is considered separately. The text then explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa says the cause (weight/depth) is single, so the death is attributed to that single cause and divided according to ownership. The Sahibayn see the loss as occurring in two distinct portions. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqila): The male agnates of the perpetrator who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on his behalf in cases of unintentional killing or injury.
دية (diyah): Blood money or financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of unintentional killing or injury.
الطلب (al-talab): The request for demolition of the wall made by one owner.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause (here: the weight of the wall or depth of the well) to which death is attributed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what fraction of diyah does the 'aqila of the requesting owner pay when a shared wall collapses?
A) 1/5
B) 1/2
C) 2/3
D) Full diyah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school diverges on whether the 'illa of the collapse is single or multiple, leading to different 'aqila shares (1/5 vs 1/2), illustrating how usul principles directly affect fiqh rulings.

Rider's Liability for Animal-Caused Injuries
Defines rider's liability for animal's actions, with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule for rider liability for animal actions, identify avoidable vs. unavoidable actions, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument using the principle of avoidability.

Core Idea
A rider is liable for avoidable animal actions but not for unavoidable kicks – the condition of safety applies only where prevention is possible.

This passage opens the chapter on injuries caused by animals (jinaayat al-bahiima) and establishes the core Hanafi principle: the rider is liable for harm caused by his mount's actions that he can control, such as trampling, biting, or striking with front limbs. He is not liable for a backward kick (nafha) with the hind leg or tail because such an action is impossible to prevent while riding. This distinction is challenged by Imam al-Shafi'i, who attributes all animal actions to the rider. We will analyze the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and the practical consequence of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions that make the rider liable
List the five actions mentioned in the text: trampling (wat'), hitting with hand/leg/head (yad/rijl/ra's), biting (kadm), striking (khabt), and colliding (sadam). These are all front-oriented and controllable.
The text uses 'kadm' for biting specifically by a donkey's front teeth.
2
Identify the exception and its rationale
The exception is the kick (nafha) with the hind leg or tail. The Hanafi reason: avoidance of this action is impossible while riding, so the condition of safety (shart al-salama) does not apply.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The principle is that public roads are shared, and passage is permissible only when conditioned by safety from avoidable harm. For unavoidable actions, the condition is lifted to keep the road open.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the rider liable for the kick as well, arguing that the animal's action is attributed to the rider as the controller. The Hanafi response rebuts that attribution is not absolute; impossibility of avoidance overrides it.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (valid disagreement).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب جناية البهيمة وعليها
ضَمِنَ الرَّاكِبُ ما وطئت دابته وما أصابت بيدها ، أو رجلها، أو رأسها، أو كدمت ، أو خبطت أو صدمت، لا ما نفحت برجلها، أو ذنبها باب جناية البهيمة" وعليها

كتاب الديات / أنواعها وأحكامها
٥٢٣
أو عطب إنسان بما راثت أو بالت في الطريق سائرة، أو أوقفها لذلك، فإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن ، فإن أصابت بيدها ، أو رجلها حصاةً أو نواة، أو أثارت غباراً أو جراً صغيراً، فَفَقأ عيناً أو أفسد ثوباً لا يضمن، وضمن بالكبير
(أو عطب إنسان بما راثت أو بالت في الطريق سائرة، أو أوقفها لذلك، فإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن)، فإنَّها إن راثت أو بالت في الطريق حالةَ السَّيرِ لا يضمن ، ، أَمَّا إذا أوقفها لتروث أو تبول لا يضمن أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ بعضَ الدَّوابِّ لَا يفعل ذلك إلا بعد الوقوف، وإن أوقفها لغير ذلك يضمن لأنه متعد بالإيقاف. (فإن أصابت بيدها ، أو رجلها حصاةً أو نواة ، أو أثارت غباراً أو حجراً صغيراً ، فَفَقأ عيناً أو أفسد ثوباً لا يضمن، وضمن بالكبير) ؛ لأن الاحتراز عن الأول متعدّرٌ بخلاف الثاني.
لأن في الوجه الأوّل صاحب البداية مباشر للإتلاف؛ لأنّ ثقله وثقل الدابـة تـصـلُ بالمتلف، فكأنهما وطئاه جميعاً ؛ ولهذا يجب على الراكب الكفارة إذا وطأت الدابة برجلها أو بيدها، ويحرم عن الميراث، والمباشر ضامن سواء كان متعدّياً أو لا. كذا في «الكفاية»(۱).

Chapter on injury by an animal and liability upon it. The rider is liable for what his animal tramples and what it hits with its hand, or its leg, or its head, or bites, or strikes, or collides, but not for what it kicks with its hind leg or its tail. Chapter on injury by an animal and liability upon it. (The rider is liable for what his animal tramples and what it hits with its hand, or its leg, or its head, or bites, or strikes, or collides (3), but not for what it kicks (1) with its hind leg or its tail), because avoidance of trampling and what resembles it is possible, unlike the kick with the hind leg and tail. This is our position. And according to al-Shafi'i (2): he is liable for the kick as well, because its action is attributed to the rider. (1) His statement: Chapter on injury by an animal... etc.; after finishing the exposition of rulings on injury by a human, he began exposition of injury by an animal, and there is no doubt that human injury takes precedence over animal injury. Thus in al-Nihaya. But it may be objected that he did not finish exposition of human injury absolutely; rather there remains injury by a slave, and there is no doubt that he is human, so it should precede animal injury, and it would have been proper to present it before animal injury, so let this be considered. [2] His statement: the rider is liable for what it tramples; trample (wati'a) with fatha: stepping on. Al-kadm: biting with the front of the human, like a donkey bites. Al-khabt (with khā'): striking with the hand. Al-sadam: is that you strike something with your body, and from it is 'the two horsemen collided' when one struck the other with his own person. It is said: the animal kicked (nafahat) something: if it struck with the edge of its hoof, thus in al-Sihah and al-Mughrib. [3] His statement: the rider is liable up to his statement: collides; intended when the rider is traveling in the Muslims' road; because if he is traveling in his own property, if it tramples with its hand or leg he is liable, and if it bites or kicks with its hand or hind leg, or strikes with its hand, there is no liability. (1) Nafahat bi-rijliha or yadiha: it struck. See Tulbat al-Talaba (p.168). (2) See al-Umm (7:158) and others. (3) al-Mughrib (p.471). Book of Blood-Monies / Types and Rulings 523 Or if a person is harmed by what it excreted or urinated in the road while traveling, or if he stopped it for that purpose, but if he stopped it for another purpose he is liable. Then if it hit with its hand or leg a pebble or a date-pit, or raised dust or a small stone, and it blinded an eye or ruined a garment, he is not liable; but he is liable for a large one. (Or if a person is harmed by what it excreted or urinated in the road while traveling, or if he stopped it for that purpose; if he stopped it for another purpose he is liable). If it excreted or urinated in the road while traveling, he is not liable. But if he stopped it for it to excrete or urinate, he is also not liable, because some animals only do that after stopping. And if he stopped it for another purpose, he is liable because he is transgressing by stopping. (Then if it hits with its hand or leg a pebble or a date-pit, or raised dust or a small stone, and it blinded an eye or ruined a garment, he is not liable; but he is liable for a large one) because avoidance of the first is impossible, unlike the second. Because in the first case, the owner of the beginning is directly causing destruction; because his weight and the animal's weight both reach the destroyed object, as if they both trampled it; and for this reason the rider must perform expiation if the animal tramples with its leg or hand, and he is prevented from inheritance, and the direct agent is liable whether he is transgressing or not. Thus in al-Kifaya. The principle is that passage in the Muslims' road is permissible but conditioned by safety; it is not an absolute right from one perspective, and from another perspective it is a right of others; because it is shared among all people. So we say: it is permissible conditioned by safety, to balance consideration from both sides. Then it is conditioned by safety regarding what can be guarded against, and not conditioned regarding what cannot, because that would prevent action and close the door, which is open. And avoidance of trampling and what resembles it is possible, for it is not among the necessities of travel, so we condition it with safety from it. But the kick with the hind leg and the tail, the rider cannot avoid it while traveling on the animal, so it is not conditioned by safety. Thus in al-Hidaya (2). (1) His statement: or a date-pit... etc.; al-nawa: date-pit, in Persian: وانه خر ما. [2] His statement: a small stone; meaning that which can be thrown, so it is larger than a pebble. (1) al-Kifaya (9:257). (2) al-Hidaya (4:197-198).
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling with inter-madhhab dispute. It opens the chapter on injuries caused by animals and specifically addresses the liability of a rider for harm done by his mount. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between actions the rider can reasonably avoid and those he cannot. The rider is liable for trampling, hitting with the animal's front parts, biting, striking, or colliding — all of which he can control by steering. He is not liable for a backward kick (nafha) with the hind leg or tail, because such movements are unpredictable and unavoidable while riding. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and holds the rider liable for the kick as well, arguing that the animal's action is directly attributed to the rider since he is in control. The Hanafi response, rooted in practical reasoning (istihsan), is that the condition of safety cannot be imposed where avoidance is impossible; otherwise, it would effectively prohibit riding altogether, which is a permissible and necessary activity. The passage then extends the principle to cases of excretion/urination on the road: no liability if the animal does so while moving (unavoidable) or if stopped specifically for that purpose (a natural need), but liability arises if the animal is stopped for an unrelated reason (transgression). Similarly, if the animal kicks up small objects like pebbles or dust, no liability due to unavoidability; but for large objects, liability exists because avoidance is possible. The commentary emphasizes that the direct agent (al-mubashir) is liable regardless of transgression, but the focus here is on the rider's vicarious liability based on the principle of causation and the shared nature of public roads.

Key Terms
نفحة (nafha): A kick with the hind leg or tail, typically backward.
كدم (kadm): Biting with the front teeth, as a donkey does.
خبط (khabt): Striking with the hand or front leg.
صدم (sadam): Colliding with the body, e.g. two horsemen striking each other.
وطء (wat'): Trampling underfoot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a rider not liable for harm caused by the animal's kick with its hind leg or tail?
A) Because the animal is not under the rider's control at all.
B) Because such kicks are unavoidable while riding, so the condition of safety cannot apply.
C) Because the kick is not considered a direct action of the rider.
D) Because the animal's owner is always liable instead.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes avoidable from unavoidable animal actions when determining rider liability, exempting the rider for the latter to ensure that permissible riding is not burdened with impossible conditions.

Diyat — Blood Money

Liability, Expiation, and Inheritance: Driver, Leader, and Rider
Ruling on guarantee, expiation, and inheritance deprivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on daman, kaffarah, and inheritance deprivation for the driver, leader, and rider, and distinguish between direct and indirect causation.

Core Idea
Direct killer pays kaffarah and loses inheritance; indirect causes share diyah but not religious penalties.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a nuanced ruling in diyat: when an animal kills someone, three people may be involved—the rider, the driver, and the leader. The Hanafi school assigns different legal consequences to each based on the nature of their causal connection to the death. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of diyah, kaffarah, and inheritance deprivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actors
The passage mentions three roles: the rider (rakib), the driver (sa'iq), and the leader (qa'id). Understand who each is: the rider is directly on the animal; the driver walks behind or controls from a distance; the leader leads the animal from the front.
2
Recognize common liability for diyah
All three are liable (damim) to pay the diyah (blood money) if the animal kills someone. The amount is the same for each—the full diyah. This is because each played a causal role in the killing, albeit with different degrees of directness.
3
Distinguish kaffarah obligation
Kaffarah (expiation for taking a life) is wajib only on the rider. The driver and leader are not obligated. The reason: the rider is the direct cause (mubashir) of the death, while the driver and leader are indirect causes (sabab).
Hanafi principle: mubashir bears religious obligations; sabab bears only financial compensation.
4
Note inheritance deprivation
If the rider is an heir of the victim, he is deprived of inheriting from the victim. The driver and leader are not deprived. This follows the same logic: the direct killer loses inheritance rights, not the indirect causes.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The passage illustrates the Hanafi distinction between mubasharah (direct causation) and tasabbub (indirect causation). The direct actor bears both financial and religious consequences; the indirect actor bears only financial liability.
This principle is used throughout Hanafi fiqh in cases of murder, manslaughter, and property damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضمن السائق والقائد ما ضمنه الراكب، وعليه الكفارة لا عليهما) : أي إن كان مكان الراكب سائق أو قائد يضمن كل منهما ما ضمنه الراكب، ويجب على الراكب الكفارة، لا على السائق والقائد والراكب يحرم عن الميراث، لا القائد والسائق.
And the driver and leader guarantee what the rider guarantees, and the kaffarah is on him (the rider), not on them. That is, if in the place of the rider there is a driver or leader, each of them guarantees what the rider guarantees, and the kaffarah is obligatory on the rider, not on the driver and leader. And the rider is deprived of inheritance, not the driver and leader.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on liability for damages caused by an animal. The driver (sa'iq) and leader (qa'id) are considered indirect causes (sabab) while the rider (rakib) is a direct cause (mubashir). Therefore, all three are liable for the diyah (blood-money) if the animal kills someone, but the kaffarah (expiation for taking a life) is only on the rider because direct causation triggers this religious obligation. Additionally, if the rider is an heir of the deceased, he is deprived of inheritance as a penalty, but the driver and leader are not deprived since they are only indirect causes. This follows the Hanafi principle that direct causation is the basis for kaffarah and inheritance deprivation.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation (diyah) for damage caused by one's action.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for taking a life, required from the direct killer.
ميراث (mirath): Inheritance; deprivation from inheriting from the victim due to causing death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true?
A) The driver, leader, and rider all must pay kaffarah.
B) The driver and leader must pay kaffarah, but the rider does not.
C) Only the rider must pay kaffarah.
D) Neither the driver, leader, nor rider must pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, direct causation (mubasharah) triggers both financial and religious consequences, while indirect causation (tasabbub) triggers only financial liability.

Diyat — Blood Money

Diyah for Colliding Horsemen: Hanafi Full Liability vs Shafi'i Half
Hanafi vs Shafi'i diyah liability for mutual collision deaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on diyah division in a collision case, explain the Hanafi reasoning that a mubah act is attributed only to harm of others, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A permissible act causing death is fully attributed to the other's death, not to one's own.

When two horsemen collide and both die, who pays diyah (blood-money) and how much? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi view against al-Shafi'i's. The core lies in how a mubah (permissible) act relates to liability — specifically, whether the victim's own act reduces the compensation.

Guided Path
1
State the case and the Hanafi ruling
Two horsemen collide and both die. According to the Hanafi school, the 'aqila (tribal guarantors) of each horseman must pay the full diyah for the other horseman.
Full diyah means the complete compensation for a life, typically 100 camels or equivalent.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its reasoning
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that each 'aqila pays only half the diyah of the other. The reasoning: each death is caused by two acts — the victim's own act and the other's act. Therefore, half is waived (from oneself) and half is considered (from the other).
The Shafi'i view reduces liability because the victim's own permissible act contributed to his death.
3
Analyse the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: each act is permissible (mubah). A mubah act is not attributed to causing one's own death (so no waiver) but is fully attributed to causing another's death. Hence full liability remains.
The key principle: attribution of a mubah act differs when considering oneself versus another.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Hanafi: each 'aqila pays full diyah → total compensation is two full diyahs. Shafi'i: each 'aqila pays half diyah → total compensation is one full diyah (half from each side).
This thamarah al-khilaf (practical outcome) can involve significant financial difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضمن عاقلة كل فارس دية الآخر إن اصطدما وماتا) : هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي: يضمن كل نصف دية الآخر؛ لأنَّ هلاكَهُ بفعلَيْن، فعل نفسه، وفعل صاحبه، فيُهْدَر نصفه ويعتبر نصف صاحبه، قلنا: فعل كل منهما مباح، والمباح في حق نفسه لا يضاف إليه الهلاك، وفي حق غيره يضاف.
And the 'aqila of each horseman guarantees the blood-money of the other if they collide and die. This is our (Hanafi) view. According to al-Shafi'i: each guarantees half of the blood-money of the other, because his destruction is by two acts: his own act and his companion's act, so half is waived and half is considered from his companion. We said: the act of each is permissible (mubah), and the permissible act in respect to oneself is not attributed to causing death, but in respect to another it is attributed.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Hanafi ruling: the 'aqila (tribal guarantors) of each horseman must pay the full diyah (blood-money) for the other horseman if both die in a collision. Shafi'i ruling: each 'aqila pays only half the diyah of the other, reasoning that each horseman's death is caused by two acts: his own act of riding (which is permissible) and the other's act. Thus, half of his life is 'waived' (not compensated) because his own act contributed. Hanafi responds: each act is permissible (mubah). A permissible act does not cause death in relation to oneself (so no waiver), but it does cause death in relation to another (so full liability). Hence, full diyah is due.

Key Terms
عاقلة (aqila): The group of male relatives of the victim who collectively pay blood-money (diyah) on behalf of the killer.
فارس (faris): Horseman, rider of a horse.
باح (mubah): Permissible act, neither commanded nor prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what diyah is due when two horsemen collide and both die?
A) Each 'aqila pays full diyah for the other
B) Each 'aqila pays half diyah for the other
C) No diyah is due because both are dead
D) The surviving 'aqila pays full diyah to the deceased's family

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a permissible act (mubah) causing harm to another imposes full liability; no reduction for the victim's own permissible act.

Liability of Animal Driver and Leader for Accidents
Ruling statement on liability for animal accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a driver or leader causes an animal accident and identify the conditions that trigger liability.

Core Idea
Negligence in controlling an animal makes the driver/leader liable for resulting death.

This passage from Al-Hidayah outlines several scenarios where a driver or leader of an animal is held liable for accidental death. The key principle is that liability arises from negligence (ta'addi) and direct causation. Even a permissible act can become a cause for guarantee if it harms another without their fault. We will explore these cases to understand the Hanafi position on diyat for animal accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Parties
First, identify the two main actors: the driver (sā'iq) who rides or drives the animal, and the leader (qā'id) who leads a caravan. Note the different scenarios: equipment falling, camel stepping, and a tied camel killing without knowledge.
2
Determine the Ruling for Each Scenario
For each scenario, state whether the driver/leader is liable (wajib) or not. The driver is liable if equipment falls due to negligence; the leader is liable if his camel steps on someone; if both are present, they are jointly liable. The exception: a leader is not liable if a tied camel kills without his knowledge.
Liability is always conditional on the actor being muta'addi (negligent).
3
Extract the Underlying Principle
The passage concludes with a general rule: a permissible act can become a cause for guarantee if it harms another. This is illustrated by the analogy of a sleeping person who rolls over and kills someone. The principle is that causation combined with the ability to prevent harm creates liability.
This principle is known as 'al-fi'l al-mubāḥ sabab li al-ḍamān'.
4
Apply the Conditions for Liability
List the conditions: 1) The actor must be muta'addi (transgressing by negligence or failure to act). 2) The act must directly cause the death. 3) The victim must be innocent. If the actor lacked knowledge (like the leader unaware of the tied camel), liability is negated.
The condition of 'ilm (knowledge) is crucial; ignorance can exempt.
5
Distinguish from Similar Cases
Compare the case of the tied camel with the case of a pedestrian falling into an unknown well. In both, the victim's own negligence or lack of knowledge of the danger exempts the owner. This shows that liability is not strict but based on fault.
The example of the well is used to clarify that the leader's lack of knowledge is analogous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسائق دابة وقع أداتها على رجل فمات وقائد قطار وطئ بعير منه رجلا، ضمن الدية وإن كان معه سائق ضمنا، فإن قتل بعير ربط على قطار بلا علم قائده رجلا كالماشي إذا لم يعلم بالبئر وقع فيها لا يهدر شيء من دمه، وفعل صاحبه وإن كان مباحا لكن الفعل المباح في غيره سبب للضمان، كالنائم إذا انقلب على غيره.
And a driver of an animal whose equipment fell on a man and he died, and a leader of a caravan whose camel stepped on a man, he guarantees the blood-money, and if there was a driver with him, they both guarantee. If a camel tied to a caravan killed a man without the knowledge of its leader, he is like a walker who falls into a well without knowing it; nothing of his blood is waived. And the act of his companion, even if permissible, is a cause for guarantee in relation to another, like a sleeping person who turns over onto another.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage provides several cases of liability for drivers and leaders of animals. (1) If a driver's saddle or other equipment falls off and kills someone, the driver is liable because he was negligent in securing the equipment. (2) A leader of a caravan whose camel steps on a person is liable because he is responsible for controlling the animals. (3) If there is both a driver and a leader, they are jointly liable. (4) If a camel tied to a caravan kills someone without the leader's knowledge, the leader is not liable (like a pedestrian who falls into an unknown well). The principle is that a permissible act can be a cause for guarantee if it harms another, analogous to a sleeping person who rolls over and kills someone. The commentary clarifies that guarantee in these cases is based on causation and negligence.

Key Terms
أداة (adat): Equipment of an animal such as saddle, reins, etc.
قطار (qitar): A line of camels tied together in a caravan.
متعدي (muta'addi): Transgressor, one who acts beyond what is permissible or fails in duty.
دية (diyah): Blood-money, compensation paid to the victim's heirs for accidental death or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a case where a driver's animal's saddle falls and kills a person, who is liable?
A) The driver
B) The rider
C) No one, it was an accident
D) The owner of the animal

Key Takeaway
A driver or leader of an animal is liable for accidental death only if he was negligent (muta'addi) and his action directly caused the harm; no liability applies if he lacked knowledge or control.

Liability for Deaths Caused by Animals: Negligence and Causation
Rulings on liability for deaths caused by animals in a caravan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a driver or leader of a caravan is liable for blood-money (diya) and apply the Hanafi principle of causation (tasbīb) to scenarios involving animal-related deaths.

Core Idea
Harm caused by a permissible action becomes liable if it results from negligence or transgression in causation.

This lesson examines the ruling on liability for deaths caused by animals in a caravan, as presented in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The key principle is that negligence in securing animals or their gear makes the handler liable for blood-money (diya), with the payment falling on the 'āqila (tribal group) rather than the individual. We will work through the scenarios of a driver whose tool falls, a leader whose camel steps, joint liability, and the special case of a tied camel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the responsible party and their duty
The driver (sāʾiq) controls the animal's direction and gear; the leader (qāʾid) controls the overall caravan. Both have a duty of care to prevent harm.
The driver is responsible for the animal's immediate control; the leader for the caravan as a whole.
2
Establish causation and negligence
Determine if the harm resulted from a failure in the duty (e.g., unsecured tool, lack of supervision). The act itself may be permissible, but negligence transforms it into a transgression (taʿaddī) in causation (tasbīb).
Liability only arises if there is negligence; unavoidable acts (like a cloak falling) incur no liability.
3
Apply the principle of tasbīb
In Hanafi law, indirect causation with negligence incurs liability. For loss of life, diya is on the 'āqila; for property, on the individual's wealth.
Tasbīb is distinguished from direct action (mubāshara).
4
Handle special scenarios
Joint liability when both driver and leader are present; the tied camel case where the leader's 'āqila pays and recovers from the tier's 'āqila; the rule for sending dogs/birds.
The time of tying (moving vs stationary) affects recovery rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : وسائق دابة ؛ معطوف عليه، وقائد قطار معطوف ؛ مبتدأ، وضمن ؛ خبره ؛ أي سائق دابـة وقـع أداتها إلى رجل فمات، يضمن الدية ؛ لأنه متعد في هذا التسبيب ؛ لأن الوقوع بتقصير من السائق، وهو ترك الشداد والإحكام فيه. بخلاف الرداء، يعني إذا كان لابساً رداءً فسقط عنه وعطب به إنسان أو غيره لا يضمن ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان لا يقصد حفظ اللباس عادة ، ولا يمكنه أن يمشي عرياناً، وما لا يستطاع الامتناع عنه يجعل عفواً.
[٢] قوله : أداتها ؛ أي آلاتها ؛ كالإكاف والسرج واللجام.
[۳] قوله : وقائد قطار وطئ بعير منه ؛ أي من القطار رجلاً ضمن الدية ؛ لأنَّ القائد عليه حفظ القطار، كالسائق، وقد أمكن ذلك ، فقد صار متعدّياً . بالتقصير فيه ، والتسبيب بوصف التعدي سبب الضمان، إلا أنّ ضمان النفس في التسبيب على العاقلة، وضمان المال في ماله. كذا في «الهداية».
[4]قوله : وإن كان معه ؛ أي مع القائد سائق، ضمنا ؛ أي القائد والسائق ؛ لأنَّ قائد الواحد قائد للكل، وكذا سائقه ؛ لاتصال الأزمة، قال في «الهداية : هذا إذا كان السائق في جانب من الإبل، أما إذا كان السائق في وسط القطار، وأخذ زمام واحدٍ ضَمِنَ عاقلةُ القائدِ الدِّية، ورجعوا بها على عاقلةِ الرّابط ) ؛ لأن الرابط أوقعهم في هذه العهدة.
أقول : ينبغي أن تكونَ في مال الرابط ؛ لأنَّ الرَّابط أوقعَهُم في خسران المال ، وهذا مما لا يتحملُهُ العاقلة ) .
قالوا: هذا إذا ربط ، والقطارُ في السَّير؛ لأنه أمرَ بالقَوَدِ دلالة ، أما إذا ربط في غير حالة السير فالضمان على عاقلة القائد ؛ لأنَّهُ قادَ بعير غيره بغيرِ أمرِه لا صريحا ولا دلالة فلا يرجعُ بما لحقه من الضمان.
( ومن أرسل كلباً أو طيراً أو ساقه (۲) ضمن ما هلك بما هو . خلفه، ويضمنان ما تلف بما بين يدي السائق ؛ لأنَّ القائد لا يقود ما خلف السائق ، والسائق يسوق ما يكون قدامه.
A driver of an animal whose tool fell on a man and he died, and a leader of a caravan whose camel trod on a man, both owe the blood-money even if there is a driver with him, they both owe. And if a camel tied to a caravan without the knowledge of its leader kills a man like a walker who does not know about a well and falls into it, nothing of his blood is wasted, and the action of its owner, even if permissible, but a permissible action in others is a cause for liability, like a sleeping person who turns over onto another. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] He said: 'driver of an animal' is conjoined, 'leader of a caravan' is conjoined, 'owes' is the predicate; i.e., a driver of an animal whose tool falls on a man and he dies owes blood-money because he becomes transgressor in this causation because the fall is due to the driver's negligence, which is leaving the tightening and fastening. Unlike the cloak, meaning if one is wearing a cloak and it falls off and a person or other is destroyed by it, he does not owe because a person does not normally intend to preserve clothing, and cannot walk naked, and what cannot be avoided is pardoned. [2] He said: 'its tool' i.e., its implements like the packsaddle, saddle, and bridle. [3] He said: 'and a leader of a caravan whose camel trod on a man' i.e., from the caravan, he owes blood-money because the leader must preserve the caravan like the driver, and that is possible, so he becomes transgressor by negligence therein, and causation with the attribute of transgression is a cause for liability, except that liability for life in causation is on the 'āqila, and liability for property is on his own wealth. Thus in al-Hidayah. [4] He said: 'and if there is with him' i.e., with the leader a driver, they both owe i.e., the leader and the driver because the leader of one is leader of all, and likewise its driver due to the connection of reins. He said in al-Hidayah: this is when the driver is at the side of the camels, but if the driver is in the middle of the caravan and took one rein, the 'āqila of the leader owes the blood-money, and they recover it from the 'āqila of the tier because the tier caused them to incur this liability. I say: it should be from the wealth of the tier because the tier caused them loss of wealth, and this is not borne by the 'āqila. They said: this is when he tied while the caravan is moving because he implicitly ordered the driving. But if he tied while not in motion, liability is on the leader's 'āqila because he drove another's camel without his order, neither explicit nor implicit, so he does not recover the liability he incurred. (And whoever sent a dog or bird or drove it (2) is liable for what perishes by what is behind him, and they both are liable for what perishes in front of the driver because the leader does not lead what is behind the driver, and the driver drives what is in front of him.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah presents rulings on liability for deaths caused by animals in a caravan under the chapter of diyāt (blood money). The matn (core text) states three scenarios: (1) A driver whose animal's tool falls on a person, (2) a leader whose camel steps on a person, and (3) the combination of both driver and leader. In each case, the person responsible owes the blood-money (diya). The commentary clarifies that this liability arises due to negligence (taqsīr) in securing the animal or its gear, making the act one of transgression (ta'addī) in causation (tasbīb). An exception is made for a cloak falling off: if a person's cloak falls and causes harm, there is no liability because one cannot avoid wearing a cloak. The principle is that permissible actions become liable when they cause harm through negligence. For joint liability, if both a driver and leader are present, they are both liable. The text then discusses a special case where a camel is tied to the caravan without the leader's knowledge: the leader's 'āqila (tribal group) pays the diya first, then recovers from the tier's 'āqila. The author (perhaps Ṣadr al-Sharī'a) opines that recovery should be from the tier's personal wealth, not the 'āqila, because it is a loss of property, not criminal liability. The time of tying matters: if tied while moving, the tier implicitly authorized driving; if tied while stationary, the leader is solely liable because he drove without permission. Finally, the rule for sending a dog or bird or driving it: liability follows the control—what is behind the driver is the leader's responsibility, what is in front is the driver's. Throughout, the commentary relies on al-Hidayah and applies Hanafi principles of causation (tasbīb) and transgression (ta'addī) to establish liability.

Key Terms
سائق (sāʾiq): The driver of an animal, responsible for controlling it; liable if harm occurs due to negligence in securing the animal or its gear.
قائد (qāʾid): The leader of a caravan, responsible for the overall control of the animals; liable for harm caused by the animals he leads due to failure of supervision.
تسبيب (tasbīb): Causation; an indirect cause of harm that, when accompanied by negligence or transgression, incurs liability. Contrasted with direct action (mubāshara).
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The tribal group or extended family responsible for paying diya (blood money) on behalf of the perpetrator in cases of unintentional killing or injury.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to make compensation, either by paying diya for life or damages for property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A driver is leading a camel whose saddlery (a tool) falls off and kills a passerby. Is the driver liable for the blood-money (diya)?
A) Yes, because the driver is negligent in securing the tool.
B) No, because the tool's fall was an accident beyond his control.
C) Yes, but the 'aqila pays, not the driver personally.
D) No, because the passerby should have been careful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, permissible actions that cause harm through negligence incur liability for blood-money on the 'āqila.

Liability of Aqila When a Tied Animal Is Led
Liability and recoupment between aqilas of leader and tier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the aqila of a leader can recoup diya from the aqila of a tier in Hanafi law and apply the distinction between tying while moving vs stationary.

Core Idea
Leading a tied animal: if the tier ties while the caravan moves, the leader's aqila recoups; if tied stationary, the leader's aqila bears full liability.

This lesson examines the liability for harm caused by a led animal that is tied to another. In Hanafi fiqh, the aqila (tribal surety) of the leader pays the diya initially but may recoup from the aqila of the tier depending on the circumstances. The key factor is whether the tier tied the animal while the caravan was moving or stationary, as that determines implied permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The aqila of the leader is liable for diya and can recoup from the aqila of the tier.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The tier involved the leader in liability by tying in a moving caravan, implying permission for leading.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The commentator suggests the tier should pay from his own wealth, not his aqila.
4
Distinguish the two scenarios
Tying while moving vs stationary determines recoupment right.
5
Apply to a new case
Determine liability if a farmer ties his ox to another's ox while plowing (stationary) and then leads it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضَمِنَ عاقلةُ القائدِ الدِّية، ورجعوا بها على عاقلةِ الرّابط؛ لأن الرابط أوقعهم في هذه العهدة. أقول: ينبغي أن تكونَ في مال الرابط؛ لأنَّ الرَّابط أوقعَهُم في خسران المال، وهذا مما لا يتحملُهُ العاقلة. قالوا: هذا إذا ربط، والقطارُ في السَّير؛ لأنه أمرَ بالقَوَدِ دلالة، أما إذا ربط في غير حالة السير فالضمان على عاقلة القائد؛ لأنَّهُ قادَ بعير غيره بغيرِ أمرِه لا صريحا ولا دلالة فلا يرجعُ بما لحقه من الضمان.
The aqila of the leader becomes liable for the diya, and they recoup it from the aqila of the tier because the tier involved them in this responsibility. I say: It should be from the wealth of the tier because the tier involved them in financial loss, and this is not borne by the aqila. They said: This is when the tier ties while the caravan is moving, because (the leader) commanded leading by implication. But if the tier ties while not in motion, the liability is on the aqila of the leader because he led another's camel without his permission, neither explicitly nor by implication, so he does not recoup the liability that befell him.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage clarifies the liability for harm caused by a tied animal in a caravan. The main ruling: when a tied animal is led by a leader and causes harm resulting in diya, the aqila of the leader pays the diya initially, but they can recoup that payment from the aqila of the tier. The reason: the tier caused the situation by tying the animal. However, a commentator (likely the author of the sharh) argues that the tier should pay from his own wealth (not his aqila) because it is a financial loss he caused. The text then distinguishes two scenarios: if the tier ties while the caravan is moving, the leader's act of leading is impliedly commanded by the tier (since the animal is meant to move with the caravan), so recoupment is allowed. But if the tier ties while the caravan is stationary, the leader leads without any command, so he bears full liability and cannot recoup. This is a typical Hanafi reasoning based on causation and implied command.

Key Terms
عاقلة (āqila): The male paternal relatives of the killer (clan) who are liable to pay diya in cases of accidental or quasi-intentional killing.
قائد (qā'id): The person who leads an animal (e.g., a camel) from front.
رابط (rābiṭ): The person who ties an animal (to another in a caravan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only if the tier tied the animal while the caravan was moving.
C) Only if the tier tied the animal while the caravan was stationary.
D) Never, because the leader is the direct cause.

Key Takeaway
Recoupment by the leader's aqila from the tier's aqila depends on whether the tier tied the animal while the caravan was moving (implied permission) or stationary (no permission).
