Liability for Sending a Dog or Bird: Driving as a Condition
Liability when dog or bird kills after being sent or driven

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a dog or bird causes harm after being sent, and explain the effect of driving and the intra-madhhab dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Liability for an animal's harm transfers to the sender only if he drives it; a bird cannot be driven, so no liability—but Abu Yusuf makes liability universal as precaution.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya distinguishes between a dog and a bird in liability for harm they cause. The key factor is whether the owner drove (urged) the animal. For a dog, driving transfers the act to the owner; for a bird, it is impossible. However, Imam Abu Yusuf differed and held the owner liable in all cases as a precaution, and his view became the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Examine two scenarios: sending a dog and sending a bird. The ruling is liability (daman) only for a dog that was driven, not for any bird, nor for a dog not driven.
The act is sending or driving an animal that causes harm.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The evidence is talil (reasoning) based on the nature of the animals. A dog can be physically coerced by driving, so the act transfers to the sender. A bird's body cannot withstand driving, so its driving is ineffective and it remains a free agent.
No explicit Quran or hadith is cited; the argument is qiyas-like talil.
3
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
Abu Yusuf holds the sender liable in all cases (dog or bird, driven or not) as a precaution (ihtiyat) to prevent harm.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad may hold the original view.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The later mashayikh adopted Abu Yusuf's position, making it the mufta bihi (operative ruling) despite the original view.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أرسل كلباً أو طيراً أو ساقه فأصاب في فوره ضَمِنَ في الكلب لا في الطير، ولا في كلب لم يسقه، الحاصل أنه لا يضمن في الطير ساق أو لم يَسُق، ويضمن في الكلب إن ساق وإن لم يَسُقُ لا، ففي الكلب ينتقل الفعلُ إليه بسبب السوق، وإن لم يسق لا ينتقل إليه؛ لأنه فاعل مختار، ولا يضمن في الطير إذا لم يسق، وكذا إن ساق؛ لأن بدنه لا يطيق السوق، فوجوده كعدمه. أقول: نعم؛ لا يطيق الضرب، أما سوقه فبالزجر والصياح بخلاف الصيد، فإنه يُحِلُّ الصَّيْدَ بمجرد الإرسال للضرورة، وعن أبي يوسف له: إِنَّهُ أوجب الضمان في هذا كلِّهِ احتياطاً، والمشايخ لله أخذوا بقوله.
And whoever sends a dog or a bird or drives it, and it strikes in its course, he is liable in the case of the dog but not the bird, nor in a dog he did not drive. The conclusion is that he is not liable in the bird whether driven or not, and he is liable in the dog if he drove, and if he did not drive, no. So in the dog, the action transfers to him due to driving, and if he did not drive, it does not transfer because (the dog) is a free agent. And he is not liable in the bird if he did not drive, and likewise if he drove, because its body cannot withstand driving, so its existence is like non-existence. I say: Yes, it cannot withstand physical driving, but its driving is by shouting and shouting, unlike hunting, for (sending a dog for) hunting makes the prey permissible merely by sending due to necessity. And from Abu Yusuf: He made liability obligatory in all this as a precaution, and the mashayikh took his position.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage distinguishes liability for harm caused by a dog versus a bird when sent or driven. The rule: if a person sends a dog and it kills or harms immediately in its course, he is liable only if he also drove (urged) the dog. If he merely sent it without driving, the dog is a free agent, so no liability. For a bird, the sender is never liable because a bird cannot be physically driven (its body cannot withstand coercion); its driving is merely by shouting or gestures, which is ineffective for liability. However, for hunting a bird of prey (like a falcon), sending it alone makes the catch permissible due to necessity. The text notes that Imam Abu Yusuf ruled liability in all cases as a precautionary measure, and later scholars (mashayikh) adopted his view, making it the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): Immediately upon sending, without pausing or deviating.
ساق (sāqa): To drive an animal from behind, urging it forward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the original Hanafi position, when is a person liable for harm caused by a dog he sends?
A) Only if he drives the dog.
B) Always, regardless of driving.
C) Never, because the dog is a free agent.
D) Only if the dog kills a human.

Key Takeaway
Liability for an animal's harm depends on causation; driving a dog transfers the act to the sender, but a bird cannot be driven, so original position excludes liability, though the mufta bihi is precautionary universal liability.

Liability for a Sent Dog or Bird Killing a Domestic Animal
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for sent animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a sent dog or bird kills a domestic animal, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
A dog can be driven, a bird cannot—so liability transfers only for a sent dog, yet caution requires liability in all cases.

This passage from the Book of Blood Money addresses a nuanced question: if you send a dog or a bird and it immediately kills a domestic animal, are you liable? The answer depends on whether the animal can be 'driven' (saqa). The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, with Abu Yusuf taking a more cautious position. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists weigh cause, capacity, and caution in liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the animals involved
The act is sending an animal (irsal) that immediately kills a domestic animal (e.g., a domestic duck). The two animals considered are a dog and a bird.
The ruling is about immediate killing (fawran), not after deviation.
2
State the baseline Hanafi rule (Abu Hanifa)
For a dog, the sender is liable only if he drove the dog (saqa). Without driving, the dog is a free agent, so no liability. For a bird, the sender is never liable, whether driven or not.
Driving means actively directing the animal toward the target.
3
Explain the rationale (talil)
For a dog, driving transfers the action to the sender because the dog is subject to being driven. For a bird, driving is ineffective because its body cannot bear being driven; its existence is like non-existence.
The author notes that driving a bird is merely by scaring and shouting, which does not control it.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Yusuf)
Abu Yusuf obligated liability in all these cases out of caution (ihtiyatan), meaning the sender is liable regardless of driving or the type of animal.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The later scholars (mashayikh) adopted Abu Yusuf's view, making it the operative ruling for fatwa. Thus, on the relied-upon view, the sender is liable in all cases.
This illustrates how caution (ihtiyat) can override the original qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات/ أنواعها وأحكامها
فأصاب في فوره ضَمِنَ في الكلب لا في الطير، ولا في كلب لم يسقه فأصاب في فورِهِ ضَمِنَ في الكلب لا في الطير، ولا في كلب لم يسقه)،
[١] قوله : فأصاب؛ أي قتل طيراً مملوكاً كالبط الأهلي، أو أشلى أي أخرى وحرض الكلب على رجل فعضه أو مزّق ثوبه).
[٢] قوله : في فوره ؛ أي فور الإرسال، وهو بفتح الفاء، وسكون الواو في الإرسال أن لا يميل يميناً وشمالاً. كذا في «الأكملية»
الحاصل أنه لا يضمن في الطير ساق أو لم يَسُق، ويضمن في الكلب إن ساق وإن لم يَسُقُ لا ، ففي الكلب ينتقل الفعلُ إليه بسبب السوق، وإن لم يسق لا ينتقل إليه ؛ لأنه فاعل مختار، ولا يضمن في الطير إذا لم يسق ، وكذا إن ساق ؛ لان بدنه لا يطيق السوق" ، فوجوده كعدمه.
أقول : نعم؛ لا يطيق الضرب، أما سوقه فبالزجر والصياح بخلاف الصيد، فإنه يُحِلُّ الصَّيْدَ بمجرد الإرسال للضرورة، وعن أبي يوسف له : إِنَّهُ أوجب الضمان في هذا كلّهِ احتياطاً، والمشايخ لله أخذوا بقوله.
[٣] قوله : لأنّ بدنه لا يطيق السوق ؛ ولو قال بدله أنّ مَن في الأرض لا يقدر على ما في الهواء، كصاحب التوفيق لكان أظهر وأسلم من اعتراض الشارح . هكذا في
[٤] قوله : فوجوده كعدمه ؛ ومن ثمة قالوا : لو أرسل بازياً في الحرم، فقتل صيد الحرم لا يضمن المرسل. كذا في الزَّيْلَعِي .
Book of Blood Money / Types and Rulings. He hit immediately: he is liable for the dog, not for the bird. And not for a dog he did not send; if he hits immediately, he is liable for the dog, not for the bird, and not for a dog he did not send. The summary: he is not liable for the bird whether he drove it or not, and he is liable for the dog if he drove it, but if he did not drive it, no. So with the dog, the action transfers to him due to driving; if he did not drive, it does not transfer because it is a free agent. He is not liable for the bird if he did not drive, and likewise if he drove, because its body cannot bear driving, so its existence is like non-existence. I say: Yes, it cannot bear hitting; as for its driving, it is by scaring and shouting, unlike hunting, for it makes hunting permissible by mere release out of necessity. From Abu Yusuf: He obligated liability in all of this out of caution, and the scholars took his view.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on liability when a person sends a dog or bird and it immediately kills a domestic animal (like a domestic duck). The text first states a concise ruling: if one sends a dog and it kills at the moment of release (fawran), the sender is liable; but if it is a bird, he is not liable. Likewise, if he does not send the dog, there is no liability. The commentator then expands: the summary is that for a bird, liability is never incurred whether driven or not; for a dog, liability is incurred only if driven (saqa). The reason is that driving a dog transfers the animal's action to the sender because the dog is subject to being driven; without driving, the dog is a free agent and the sender is not liable. For a bird, driving is ineffective because its body cannot bear being driven; its existence is like non-existence. The author adds a note: driving a bird is merely by scaring and shouting, which does not control it, unlike hunting where release alone suffices due to necessity. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) obligates liability in all these cases out of caution, and the later scholars (mashayikh) adopted his view, making it the mufta bihi position. Finally, a corollary: if one releases a falcon in the Haram and it kills game in the Haram, the releaser is not liable, because the falcon's action is not attributed to him.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): The immediate moment of release, without deviation right or left.
سوق (sawq): Driving the animal, i.e., actively directing it toward the target.
إرسال (irsal): Sending or releasing an animal to attack or hunt.
احتياط (ihtiyat): Legal caution, preferring the stricter ruling to avoid wrongdoing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in this passage, what is the ruling if a person sends a dog and it immediately kills a domestic duck?
A) The sender is liable, whether he drove the dog or not.
B) The sender is not liable under any circumstances.
C) The sender is liable only if he drove the dog.
D) The sender is liable only if the duck was in a sanctuary.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi view imposes liability on the sender whenever a sent dog or bird immediately kills a domestic animal, out of caution.

Liability for a Stray Animal and for Pricking a Mount
Ruling on stray animal harm and liability for pricking a mount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for harm caused by a stray animal and explain why the pricker is liable when a mounted animal is pricked.

Core Idea
A stray animal is like a wild animal — no liability; but pricking a mount transfers liability from rider to pricker.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya covers two important scenarios in the law of torts (jināyāt) concerning animals. The first is the case of a stray animal (dābba munfalita) that causes harm — the general rule is no liability, based on the prophetic hadith 'al-ʿajmāʾ jubār'. The second scenario involves a person who strikes or pricks a mounted animal, causing it to harm a third party. The Hanafi school holds the pricker solely liable, while Abu Yusuf splits liability equally. We will explore the evidence and reasoning behind these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and their rulings
What are the two cases presented? For stray animal: no liability. For pricking a mount: the pricker is liable, not the rider. Note the exception: if the pricker acted with permission, the ruling may differ (not discussed here).
The second scenario is subdivided into kicking, striking with hand, or shying and colliding.
2
Locate the dalil for the stray animal ruling
The hadith 'ar-ajmā' jubār' is cited. Identify the source and its level of authenticity. The Hanafi school considers it sufficient to establish no liability.
The hadith is from Bukhari via Abu Huraira, and is classified as ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning (dalāla) is clear (qat'i).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah for the pricking scenario
The Hanafi reasoning: the rider and mount are both propelled by the pricker's action. The pricker's force overrides, making him the direct cause. This is analogous to complete coercion (ikrāh kāmil), where the coerced person is not held liable.
The athar (reports) of Umar and Ibn Mas'ud are also cited as supporting evidence, leading to abandonment of qiyas.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf argues that the rider's weight and the animal's step contribute to the death, so liability should be shared equally. Identify his evidence and the Hanafi response.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the text affirms 'this is our view' indicating reliance on Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Why is Abu Hanifa's view preferred? The text states 'this is our view' and cites the athar. The pricker's action is the direct cause; the rider's contribution is not independent.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is full diya on the pricker versus half on each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في دابة منفلتة أصابت نفساً، أو مالاً ليلاً أو نهاراً. ومَن ضَرَبَ دابة عليها راكب، أو نَخَسَها فنفحت، أو ضربت بيدها آخر أو نفَرَتْ فصدمته وقتلته ضمن هو لا الراكب ) هذا عندنا، وعند أبي يوسف : إن الضمان على الرَّاكب والناخس نصفين، وهذا إذا نَخَسَها بلا إذن.
[١) قوله : ولا في دابةٍ منفلتة... الخ؛ أي لا ضمان في دابة منفلتة أصابت نفساً أو مالاً ليلاً أو نهاراً ، فقوله : جرح العجماء جبار رواه البخاري عن أبي هريرة ، وقال محمد الله : أي المنفلتة ; ولأنّ الفعل غير مضاف إليه ; لعدم الإرسال وأخواته.
صاحبه.
[٢]قوله : منفلتة ; مأخوذ من انفلت الشيء إذ خالص من قيده من غير اختيار صاحبه.
[٣]قوله : لا الراكب ; أي لا يضمن الراكب، فإن قيل : القياس يقتضي أن يكونَ الضمان على الراكب ; لكونه مباشراً وإن لم يكن متعدياً ; لأن التعدي ليس من شرطه ، فإن لم يختص فلا أقل من الشركة.
الجواب : إن القياس يترك بالأثر، وفيه أثرُ . عمر وابن مسعود ، وأيضاً : إنّ الراكب والمركوب مدفوعان بدفع الناخس ، والمدفوع إلى الشيء وإن كان مباشراً لا يعتبر مباشراً، كما في الإكراه الكامل، فلا يجب عليه جزاء المباشرة إن فرض مباشراً ولا التسبيب أيضاً ; لأنه يعتمد التعدّي وهو مفقود. كذا في «العناية».
[٤] قوله : هذا عندنا ; وهو المروي عن عمر وابن مسعود ; ولأن الراكب والمركب مدفوعان بدفع الفاحش ، فأضيف فعلُ الدابة إلى الناخس ، كأَنه فعله .
[٥] قوله : نصفين ; لأنَّ التلف حصلَ بثقل الراكب، ووطئ الدابة، والوطء فعل الراكب، فوجب أن يضاف إليه، فلا يبطل بفعل الناخس.
And not for a stray animal that hits a person or property at night or day. And whoever strikes an animal that has a rider, or pricks it so that it kicks, or strikes with its hand another, or shies and collides with him and kills him, he (the striker) is liable, not the rider. This is our view. And with Abu Yusuf: the liability is on the rider and the pricker half and half. This is when he pricks it without permission. Commentary: 'stray animal' means no liability, based on the hadith 'The wound of the dumb animal is of no account' (Bukhari). 'stray' means escaped from its owner involuntarily. 'not the rider' means the rider is not liable; if it is said analogy would make the rider liable as a direct agent, the answer is that analogy is abandoned for the athar (report) of Umar and Ibn Mas'ud, and also the rider and mount are both propelled by the pricker, so the propelled person, even if direct, is not considered a direct agent, like in complete coercion. So neither direct nor indirect liability applies. 'This is our view' is the narration from Umar and Ibn Mas'ud; because the rider and mount are propelled by the striker, so the animal's action is attributed to him. 'half and half' because the death resulted from the rider's weight and the animal's step, which is the rider's action, so it must be attributed to him, and not nullified by the pricker's action.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage covers two related issues: liability for harm caused by a stray animal, and liability when someone strikes or pricks a mounted animal causing it to harm a third party. First, there is no liability for a stray animal (dābba munfalita) that causes harm to person or property, day or night. This is based on the prophetic hadith 'Jurh al-ʿajmāʾ jubār' (The wound of the dumb animal is of no account) recorded by Bukhari. The term 'stray' means the animal has escaped its owner involuntarily; since there is no direct action by the owner (no sending etc.), no liability attaches. Second, if a person strikes or pricks an animal that has a rider, causing it to kick, strike with its hand, or shy and collide with someone and kill them, the striker (nākhis) is liable, not the rider. This is the Hanafi position (our view). Abu Yusuf holds that liability is shared equally between the rider and the pricker. The Hanafi proof includes: (a) Athar (reports) from Umar and Ibn Mas'ud supporting this; (b) The rider and mount are both propelled by the pricker's action, similar to complete coercion (ikrāh kāmil), where the coerced person is not considered a direct agent. Therefore, the rider is not liable either as a direct or indirect cause. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that the death also involves the rider's weight and the animal's step, which is an action of the rider, so he should share liability.

Key Terms
منفلتة (munfalita): An animal that has escaped its owner's control without the owner's choice.
نخس (nakhs): To prick or poke an animal to make it move.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position (our view), if a person pricks a horse that has a rider, and the horse kicks and kills a passerby, who is liable?
A) The rider only.
B) The pricker only.
C) Both equally.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
No liability for stray animals; full liability on the pricker of a mounted animal.

No Liability for a Stray Animal
Ruling on stray animal liability with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that no liability attaches to the owner of a stray animal and identify the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A stray animal’s injury is not compensated—the owner is free of liability.

The matn opens with a clear rule: there is no liability (daman) for a stray animal that causes harm to person or property, at any time. This ruling is drawn from the prophetic hadith 'Jarh al-ajma jabbar'. In this lesson, we will unpack the proof and the rationale behind this exemption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states 'Wa la fi dabbatin munfalitat...' meaning there is no liability for injury caused by a stray animal. Note the comprehensive scope: includes injury to a person (nafs) and property (mal), both night and day.
2
Isolate the dalil
The commentary cites the hadith 'Jarh al-ajma jabbar' (the wound of the dumb animal is of no account). This hadith is the textual proof that the owner is not liable.
Record that the hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari, so it is a sound hadith.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'ajma' refers to a stray animal that has escaped. The phrase 'jabbar' means that no compensation is due. Thus the hadith directly establishes the ruling that no liability falls on the owner for the stray animal's acts.
The commentary also mentions that the act is not attributed to the owner because he did not deliberately release the animal.
4
Understand the scope
The ruling applies whether the injury occurs at night or day, and whether it is to a person or property. The stray animal is defined as one that has escaped without the owner's choice (infilat).
This definition ensures that the owner is not at fault.

[٤] قوله : هذا عندنا ؛ وهو المروي عن عمر وابن مسعود ؛ ولأن الراكب والمركب مدفوعان بدفع الفاحش ، فأضيف فعلُ الدابة إلى الناخس ، كأَنه فعله . And there is no liability for a stray animal that injures a person or property, night or day. And whoever strikes an animal that has a rider, or prods it, and it kicks, or strikes with its hand another, or it bolts and collides with someone and kills him, he (the striker/prodder) is liable, not the rider. (The matn repeats with slight variation) This is our view. According to Abu Yusuf, the liability is upon the rider and the prodder in halves, and this is if he prods without permission. Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah with commentary from al-Hidayah explaining liability for animal injuries. The passage first states that no liability attaches to the owner of a stray animal that causes harm, based on the hadith 'العجماء جبار'. Then it addresses a specific scenario: if a person strikes or prods an animal that has a rider, and that animal reacts by kicking or running into someone, killing him, the prodder is liable, not the rider. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. Abu Yusuf differs: he holds both the rider and the prodder liable in equal halves, because the injury resulted from the weight of the rider as well as the prodding. The commentary explains that the analogy would place liability on the rider as the direct agent, but this analogy is abandoned in favor of the athar (reports) from Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. Furthermore, the rider and the mount are both being propelled by the prodder, similar to duress, so the rider is not considered a direct cause. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
منفلتة (munfalita): A stray animal that has escaped from its owner's control without choice.
جبار (jabbar): Liable for no compensation; the injury is disregarded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The owner is not liable.
D) The animal itself is liable.

Key Takeaway
A stray animal (munfalita) that causes harm does not incur liability on its owner, as established by the hadith and Hanafi consensus.

Liability for Prodding a Ridden Animal: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for prodding a ridden animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when a person prods a ridden animal and it causes harm, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The prodder bears full liability; the rider is not liable because both are driven by the prodder.

When a third party prods or strikes a ridden animal and it reacts by kicking or running into someone, who is liable? Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold the prodder solely responsible, while Abu Yusuf divides the liability equally between rider and prodder. This lesson unpacks the proofs and reasoning behind each view and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
The matn describes: a person strikes or prods an animal with a rider; the animal kicks or bolts and kills someone. The text immediately rules that the striker/prodder is liable, not the rider.
2
Identify the positions
The commentary notes that according to 'us' (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) the prodder alone is liable; according to Abu Yusuf, the liability is split half on the rider and half on the prodder.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that both the rider's weight (thiqal) and the prodding contributed to the injury, so liability should be shared.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The commentary rebuts that the rider and the mount are both driven by the prodder, similar to duress (ikrah kamil). Therefore the rider is not a direct cause (mubashir) and cannot be held liable.
The proof includes athar from Umar and Ibn Mas'ud.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: full liability on the prodder.
This is the position found in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في دابة منفلتة أصابت نفساً، أو مالاً ليلاً أو نهاراً. ومَن ضَرَبَ دابَّةٌ عليها راكب، أو نَخَسَها فنفحت، أو ضربت بيدها آخر، أو نفَرَتْ فصدمته وقتلته ضمنَ هو لا الراكب (ولا) في دابة منفلتة " أصابت نفساً، أو مالاً ليلاً أو نهاراً. ومَن ضَرَبَ دابة عليها راكب، أو نَخَسَها فنفحت، أو ضربت بيدها آخر أو نفَرَتْ فصدمته وقتلته ضمن هو لا الراكب ) (٣) ، هذا عندنا، وعند أبي يوسف إن الضمان على الرَّاكب والناخس نصفين ، وهذا إذا نَخَسَها بلا إذن [١) قوله : ولا في دابةٍ منفلتة... الخ؛ أي لا ضمان في دابة منفلتة أصابت نفساً أو مالاً ليلاً أو نهاراً ، فقوله : جرح العجماء جبار (۱) رواه البخاري عن أبي هريرة ، وقال محمد الله : أي المنفلتة ؛ ولأنّ الفعل غير مضاف إليه ؛ لعدم الإرسال وأخواته. صاحبه. And there is no liability for a stray animal that injures a person or property, night or day. And whoever strikes an animal that has a rider, or prods it, and it kicks, or strikes with its hand another, or it bolts and collides with someone and kills him, he (the striker/prodder) is liable, not the rider. (The matn repeats with slight variation) This is our view. According to Abu Yusuf, the liability is upon the rider and the prodder in halves, and this is if he prods without permission. [Commentary: Explanation of the matn, the hadith 'Jarh al-ajma' jabbar' from Bukhari, the definition of munfalita, the objection that analogy would make the rider liable, and the answer that analogy is left for the athar, and the rider and mount are both driven by the prodder, so the act is attributed to the prodder, like in coercion. The view of Abu Yusuf is explained as half liability because damage occurred from the rider's weight and the animal's stepping.]
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah with commentary from al-Hidayah explaining liability for animal injuries. The passage first states that no liability attaches to the owner of a stray animal that causes harm, based on the hadith 'العجماء جبار'. Then it addresses a specific scenario: if a person strikes or prods an animal that has a rider, and that animal reacts by kicking or running into someone, killing him, the prodder is liable, not the rider. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. Abu Yusuf differs: he holds both the rider and the prodder liable in equal halves, because the injury resulted from the weight of the rider as well as the prodding. The commentary explains that the analogy would place liability on the rider as the direct agent, but this analogy is abandoned in favor of the athar (reports) from Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. Furthermore, the rider and the mount are both being propelled by the prodder, similar to duress, so the rider is not considered a direct cause. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
نخس (nakhs): To prod or poke an animal, typically with a stick, considered a direct act leading to liability for the consequences.
إكراه كامل (ikrah kamil): Full duress where a person is compelled to act without any choice, absolving them of liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The rider only
B) The prodder only
C) Both equally
D) The owner of the animal

Key Takeaway
When a ridden animal is prodded by a third party and causes harm, the prodder bears full liability; the rider is exempt because he is driven by the prodder.

Diya for Poking Out the Eye of a Slaughtered Animal
Ruling on diya for eye injury to domestic animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diya for poking out the eye of sheep versus work animals (cow, camel, donkey, mule, horse) and explain the illah (reason) based on the animal's use.

Core Idea
For work animals, diya is one-fourth the value; for meat animals, it is the diminution in value.

This passage from al-Hidayah provides a clear distinction in compensatory liability (diya) for injuring the eye of domestic animals. The ruling hinges on whether the animal is primarily used for meat or for work. We will unpack the ruling, the rationale, and the owner's options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of animals
List the animals mentioned: sheep, cow, camel, donkey, mule, horse. Note that the text divides them into two groups: sheep alone receives the diminution in value; all others receive one-fourth of value.
2
State the ruling for each group
For the sheep (butcher's sheep): compensation is what it diminishes in value (ma naqasaha). For cow, camel, donkey, mule, horse: one-fourth of the value (rub' al-qima).
3
Extract the illah (legal reason)
The text gives the illah for the work animals: 'because it is possible to perform work with them using four eyes: its two eyes and the two eyes of the user.' This indicates that their utility remains largely intact.
4
Understand the owner's option for the sheep
For the sheep, the owner may either keep the injured animal and claim the loss in value, or hand it over to the injurer and claim its full value. This is because the sheep is solely for meat, so the loss is subjective.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider a camel that has never been used for work but is prepared for slaughter. The text explicitly says the ruling is still one-fourth, not the diminution, because the illah is based on the potential for work, not actual use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of Diyat: Types and Rulings. And in poking out the eye of a butcher's sheep, (the compensation is) what it diminishes in value. And in the eye of a butcher's cow, and his camel prepared for slaughter, and the donkey, the mule, and the horse, one-fourth of the value. Because it is possible to perform work with them using four eyes: its two eyes and the two eyes of the user. [Commentary: Explanation of 'what it diminishes': it is valued with a healthy eye and then with a lost eye, and the difference is paid, including the depreciation from emaciation. Since it is for meat, the owner has the option to either give the animal to the injurer and claim its full value, or keep it and claim the diminution. For the cow and camel, etc., even if prepared for slaughter, the ruling is one-fourth of the value, to prevent misunderstanding that they would be treated like sheep; rather, whether they are used for meat or for plowing/riding, the compensation is one-fourth, like animals whose meat is not eaten.]
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah provides the ruling on diya for injuring the eye of various domestic animals. For a sheep (which is primarily for meat), the compensation is the amount by which its value decreases due to the loss of the eye. This is determined by valuing the sheep before and after. The owner has the option to keep the sheep and claim the diminution, or hand it over to the injurer and claim its full value. For a cow, a camel prepared for slaughter, a donkey, a mule, or a horse, the compensation is one-fourth of the animal's value. The reason given is that these animals are used for work (like plowing, riding, carrying loads), and they can still perform their function with the remaining three eyes (their own one good eye and the user's two eyes). This rationale applies regardless of whether these animals are actually used for meat or for work; the ruling is uniform at one-fourth of the value. This differentiates them from the sheep, which is exclusively for meat and thus suffering a more significant loss in value.

Key Terms
فقء عين (faq' al-ayn): Poking out or blinding the eye of an animal.
جزور (jazur): A camel prepared for slaughter, but the legal application here extends to any camel used for work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The diminution in value
B) One-fourth of its value
C) Full value of the cow
D) One-half of its value

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between meat animals (sheep) and work animals (cow, camel, donkey, mule, horse) for diya on eye injury: the former gets the loss in value, the latter gets a fixed one-fourth of value.

Compensation for Animal Eye Injuries and Rider Liability
Rules of diyat for animal eye injuries and rider liability for kicks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on compensating for animal eye injuries and identify the distinction between meat and work animals, and apply the rule of rider liability when an animal kicks after being poked with permission.

Core Idea
Compensation for an animal's eye depends on its purpose: quarter value for work animals, decrease in value for meat animals.

This passage from the Hidayah tradition deals with two related questions in the law of torts (jinayat). First, what is due when someone pokes out an animal's eye? The answer depends on the animal's use — work or meat. Second, when a rider's animal kicks after being poked by another person with the rider's permission, who bears liability? The classical Hanafi analysis balances revealed texts, analogy, and practical distinctions. We will work through the ruling, its proof, and the Shafi'i disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify animal categories and compensation rules
Note the two categories: meat animals (sheep) get decrease in value; work animals (cow, donkey, mule, horse) get a quarter of value. The butcher's cow is considered a work animal despite being in a slaughterhouse.
The key distinction is the animal's primary purpose, not its ownership.
2
Locate the prophetic dalil
The hadith 'قضى في عين الدابة بربع القيمة' is the primary evidence for the quarter rule. It is ahad but accepted by the Hanafis as sufficient for establishing wajib compensation.
Imam al-Shafi'i does not accept this hadith as universally applicable, leading to the ikhtilaf.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i analogy
Al-Shafi'i analogizes all animals to the sheep, arguing for decrease in all cases. The Hanafi response points to the hadith and the logical consequence that poking both eyes of a work animal would require half the value, which is not established.
The Hanafi usul uses the hadith as a mukhassis (specifier) for work animals, overriding the general qiyas.
4
Apply the rider liability rule
If a person pokes an animal with the rider's permission, the rider is liable for any resulting kicks. The poke is equivalent to driving, so liability transfers to the rider. If the poke is without permission, the poker is liable.
This is based on the maxim that command to act transfers liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, compensation for a cow's eye is a quarter of its value; under Shafi'i, it would be the decrease in value. This affects actual awards in tort cases.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the real-world financial difference.

Source Text

Book of Diyat. For poking out the eye of the butcher's sheep: what it decreased. For the eye of the butcher's cow, his camel prepared for slaughter, the donkey, the mule, and the horse: a quarter of the value. As for when he pokes it with his permission, he is not liable because he commanded him with what he owns, since poking is in the meaning of driving, so liability transfers to the rider, so he is not liable for the kick, just as if the rider poked the animal and it kicked. [1] His statement: 'what it decreased': so it is valued as sound-eyed and missing an eye, so he guarantees the difference, and the decrease includes the emaciation resulting from loss of the eye. Likewise in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His statement: 'what it decreased': because it is for meat, and regarding its two eyes, its owner may choose: if he wishes, he leaves it with the poker and makes him liable for its value, or he keeps it and makes him liable for the decrease. Likewise in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, quoting from al-Zayla'i. [3] His statement: 'the eye of the butcher's cow' meaning the butcher, and al-jazr is cutting, and jazr al-jazur is slaughtering it, which is what is prepared from camels for slaughter. He only placed the issue on the butcher's cow and his camel to prevent the misunderstanding that since they are prepared for meat, their ruling would be like the sheep; rather, whether they are prepared for that or for plowing and riding, it contains a quarter of the blood money, just like animals whose meat is not eaten, like the mule and donkey. Likewise in the commentaries of al-Hidayah, thus in al-Jalabi. And according to al-Shafi'i, the decrease must be compensated as in the butcher's sheep. [1] His statement: 'And according to al-Shafi'i, the decrease must be compensated as in the butcher's sheep': the difference is what we presented, but it is objected that if he poked the eyes of a donkey, for example, he would guarantee half its value, but it is not so as passed; so it is better to adhere to what was narrated: that he (the Prophet) judged concerning the eye of an animal a quarter of the value. And the restriction to the eye is because if he cuts its ear or tail, he guarantees its decrease. Likewise for the tongue of the bull and donkey. Likewise in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from the book of Diyat (blood money) deals with compensation for poking out the eye of animals and the liability of a rider when his animal kicks someone. The author first presents the ruling in the matn: for a butcher's sheep (which is raised for meat), the compensation is the decrease in its market value (ما نقصها). But for a butcher's cow, his camel prepared for slaughter, a donkey, a mule, and a horse, the compensation is a quarter of the animal's value. The reason is that these animals are used for work (plowing, riding) and can still function with one eye, using their own eye and the user's eye as the second. For a sheep, the only purpose is meat, so any loss of value is compensated based on the decrease. The commentary then elaborates: for the sheep, the owner has the option to either give the animal to the injurer and claim its full value or keep it and claim the decrease. For the working animals, the fixed quarter of the value is based on a prophetic ruling. The passage also discusses the case of a person who pokes an animal with the rider's permission: if the animal then kicks someone, the rider is liable, not the poker, because the poke is analogous to driving. The dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i is addressed: al-Shafi'i held that all animal eye injuries are compensated by the decrease in value, like the sheep. The Hanafi response distinguishes between meat animals (sheep) and work animals; for the latter, the quarter is established by the hadith, and if it were decrease, poking out both eyes of a donkey would require half the value, which is not the ruling. Therefore, the Hanafi position is stronger based on the revealed text.

Key Terms
فقء (faq'): Poking or gouging out (an eye).
نخس (nakhs): Poking an animal to make it move (like a prod).
نفحة (nafḥah): A kick or strike from an animal.
قصاب (qaṣṣāb): Butcher (slaughterer of sheep or cattle).
جزار (jazzār): Butcher who slaughters larger animals (cows, camels).
جزور (jazūr): A camel prepared for slaughter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Full blood money

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between meat animals (decrease) and work animals (quarter) for eye injuries, based on a prophetic hadith, and rider liability transfers when the animal is poked with permission.

Ruling on a Slave's Accidental Killing
Explaining the rule when a slave commits accidental killing and master's options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a slave's accidental killing and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding pursuing the slave after emancipation.

Core Idea
For a slave's accidental killing, the master must surrender or ransom immediately; the freed slave is not liable.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the master's liability when his slave commits an accidental killing. The Hanafi school gives the master a choice: surrender the slave to the victim's guardian or pay the ransom immediately. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, including the immediate nature of payment and the disagreement with the Shafi'i school over whether the slave can be pursued after emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the master's two options and the immediate obligation.
The master must choose: surrender the slave or pay the ransom (arsh) right away.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the obligation is on the slave himself, not the master.
According to al-Shafi'i, the slave is to be sold unless the master pays; the victim can pursue the slave after freedom.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: the crime is the slave's act, so liability attaches to the slave.
The proof is based on the direct responsibility of the slave for his actions.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain why the Hanafi school considers emancipation as a choice of ransom, clearing the slave's liability.
By freeing the slave, the master effectively ransoms him, so the victim cannot then claim from the freed slave.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: whether a freed slave can be sued for the crime.
Hanafis: no; Shafi'is: yes. This difference impacts real cases of emancipation following the crime.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب جناية الرقيق وعليه
فإن جنى عبد خطأ دفعَهُ سَيِّدُهُ بها : أي بالجناية ، ويملكه وليها
[١] قوله : باب جناية الرقيق وعليه ؛ لما فرغ من بيان جناية الحرّ على الحرّ، شرع في بيان جناية المملوك، والجناية عليه ، ولَمّا كان فيه تعلّق الملك بالمملوكِ البتة من جانب أخره ؛ لانحطاط رتبة المملوكِ عن المالك. ويرد عليه أنّ العبد لا يكون أدنى منزلة من البهيمة، فكيف أخر باب جنايته عن باب جناية البهيمة. ويجاب عنه ؛ إنَّ جناية البهيمة كانت باعتبار الراكب والسائق وغيرهما، وهم هلاك، وفيه ما فيه فليتأمل.
[٢] قوله : فإن جنى.... الخ ؛ أي إن جنى العبد جناية خطأ فالمولى إما يدفعه بالجناية، أو يفد بـه ، فإن دفعه يملكُهُ وَلِيُّ الجناية، وإن فداه فداه بأرش الجناية مالاً ، وكل ذلك يلزمه حالاً. أما الدفع ؛ فلأن التأجيل في الأعيان باطل، وعند اختيار المولى الدفع الواجب عين. وأما الفداء ؛ فلانه جعلَ بدلاً عن العبد في الشرع، وإن كان مقدراً بالمتلف ، ولهذا سمي فداءً، فيقوم مقام العبد، ويأخذ حكمه ؛ فلذا وجب حالاً كالمبدل. كذا في «الهداية»).
[٣] قوله : خطأ ؛ التقييد بالخطأ هاهنا إنّما يفيد في النفس ؛ لأنّ بعمده يقتص، وأما فيما دون النفس فلا يفيد ؛ لاستواء خطئه وعمده فيما دونها، ثمّ إنّما يثبت الخطأ بالبينة أو إقرار مولاه أو علم القاضي لا بإقراره أصلاً ، قلت : لكن قوله : أو علم القاضي غير المفتى به ؛ لأنه لا يعملُ بعلم القاضي في زماننا. كذا في «الدر المختار»).
أو فداه بأرشها حالاً، فإن فداه فجنى ، فهي كالأولى
(۱۳) : الجناية في رقبته ،
أو فداه بأرشها حالاً) (۲) ، هذا عندنا ، وعند الشَّافِعِيِّ يباع فيها إلا أن يقضي المولى الأرش، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في اتباع الجاني بعد العتق ، فإنَّ المجني عليه يتَّبِعُ الجاني إذا عتق عند ا الشَّافِعِي .
(فإن فداه فجنى ، فهي كالأولى ، فإنَّهُ إذا فدى طَهُرَ عن الأولى، فصارت الأولى كأن لم تكن، فيجب بالثانية الدفع أو الفداء )
[ 1 ] قوله : أو فداه بأرشها ... الخ ؛ فداء الشيء بالكسر، هو الذي عوّض عنه، وقائم مقامه، والأرش وإن كان اسماً لما وجب دون النفس، لكنَّ المراد هاهنا ما يقابل الجناية، ويكون عوضا عنها، سواء كانت في النفس أو فيما دونها، واستعماله في هذا المعنى الأعم في إطلاقات الفقهاء غير عديد. كذا في الجلبي .
[۲] قوله حالاً ؛ قال أخي جلبي : هذا قيد للدفع والفداء معاً، كأن هذا دفع لتوهم أن الفداء لما وجب بمقابلة الجناية في النفس أو العضو أشبه الدية والأرش، وهما يثبتان مؤجلاً، وذلك يقتضي ثبوت الفداء مؤجلاً سنةً أو ثلاث سنين ، لكن لَمَّا اختاره المولى صار في ذمّتِهِ ديناً حالاً ، كسائر ديونه ؛ لأنَّ الأجل في الديون عارض ؛ ولذا لا يثبت إلا بالشرط ، هكذا فهم من تقرير الأكمل .
[٣] قوله : وعند الشافعي ... الخ ؛ يعني أنّ عنده الوجوب على العبد، فيطالب هذا المجني عليه بعد عتقه، وعندنا الوجوب على المولى دون العبد، فلا يستسعيه بعد العتق ؛ لأنه بالإعتاق صارَ مختاراً للفداء ، قال أخي جلبي) : هذا زبدة ما في «العناية».
[٤] قوله : فيجب بالثانية الدفع أو الفداء ؛ كأنه دفع لتوهم أن المولى لا يخاطب ثانياً بأحد هذين الأمرين ؛ لأنَّ الجنايةَ إمّا في رقبة العبد يباع فيها أو غير ذلك. كذا في الجلبي.
Chapter on the crime of the slave and liability. If a slave commits an accidental killing, his master surrenders him for it: i.e., for the crime, and the legal guardian of the crime becomes his owner. [1] He said: Chapter on the crime of the slave and liability; since he finished explaining the crime of a free person against a free person, he began explaining the crime of the slave and the crime against him. And because there is [in this] absolute attachment of ownership to the slave from the other side due to the lower rank of the slave compared to the owner. It is objected that a slave is not lower in status than an animal, so why did he postpone the chapter on his crime after the chapter on the crime of an animal? It is answered: that the crime of an animal was considered by virtue of the rider, driver, and others, and they are destroyed, and there is something in it, so let it be considered. [2] He said: 'for if he commits ...' etc.; i.e., if the slave commits an accidental crime, then the master either surrenders him for the crime or ransoms him. If he surrenders him, the victim's guardian becomes his owner; if he ransoms him, he ransoms him with the compensation money of the crime as property, and all of that is immediately binding on him. As for surrender, because deferment in tangible property is invalid, and when the master chooses surrender, it is an obligatory tangible object. As for ransom, because it is made a substitute for the slave in law, although it is estimated by the damaged thing, therefore it is called ransom; it stands in place of the slave and takes his ruling; hence it is immediately obligatory like the substitute. (Quoted from al-Hidayah). [3] He said: 'accidental'; the restriction to accidental here is only effective regarding a life, because for intentional, retaliation is required; but for less than life, it is not effective because accidental and intentional are equal for less than life. Moreover, accidental is established by evidence or the master's confession or the judge's knowledge, not by the slave's confession at all. I said: but his statement 'or the judge's knowledge' is not the mufta bihi (relied upon position), because the judge's knowledge is not acted upon in our time. (Quoted from al-Durr al-Mukhtar). Or he ransoms him with its compensation immediately; if he ransoms him and then he commits a crime, it is like the first. The crime is on his neck. Or he ransoms him with its compensation immediately; this is according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i, he is sold for it unless the master pays the compensation. The fruit of the difference appears in pursuing the criminal after emancipation, for the victim pursues the criminal after emancipation according to al-Shafi'i. (If he ransoms him and he commits a crime, it is like the first; for when he ransoms him, he becomes pure from the first, so the first becomes as if it never was; thus with the second, surrender or ransom is obligatory.) [1] He said: 'or he ransoms him with its compensation, etc.'; ransom (fida') of a thing is that which is given in exchange for it and stands in its place; and compensation (arsh) although it is a name for what is obligatory for less than life, but here what is meant is that which corresponds to the crime and is a substitute for it, whether it is for a life or less than it; its use in this general sense is not infrequent in the usage of jurists. (Quoted from al-Jalabi). [2] He said: 'immediately'; my brother Jalabi said: this is a restriction for both surrender and ransom, as if it is a refutation of the misconception that since the ransom is obligatory in exchange for the crime against life or limb, it resembles blood money and compensation, which are established on deferment, thus requiring the ransom to be deferred for a year or three years, but when the master chose it, it became a debt immediately owed, like all his other debts; because deferment in debts is incidental, therefore it is not established except by condition. This is understood from the elaboration of al-Akmal. [3] He said: 'and according to al-Shafi'i, etc.'; meaning that according to him, the obligation is on the slave, so the victim demands him after his emancipation; but according to us, the obligation is on the master, not the slave, so he is not pursued after emancipation because by emancipating, he chose the ransom. My brother Jalabi said: this is the gist of what is in al-Inayah. [4] He said: 'thus with the second, surrender or ransom is obligatory'; as if it is a refutation of the misconception that the master is not addressed a second time with either of these two options, because the crime is either on the slave's neck, where he is sold for it, or not. (Quoted from al-Jalabi).
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a legal commentary (sharh) explaining the Hanafi ruling on a slave's accidental crime. The base text (matn) states: 'If a slave commits an accidental killing, his master surrenders him for it.' The commentator expands, noting that the master has a choice: either surrender the slave to the victim's guardian (who then becomes the slave's owner) or ransom the slave by paying the blood money (arsh) immediately. The payment must be immediate because deferment applies to debts, but here the master chooses a tangible surrender or a substitute, both of which are due at once. The restriction to 'accidental' is significant only for killing (where intentional killing demands retaliation), but for injuries below life, accidental and intentional are treated equally. The proof of accident is by evidence, master's confession, or judge's knowledge, but the judge's knowledge is not the operative position today. The passage then contrasts with al-Shafi'i: he holds that the slave is to be sold unless the master pays, and the victim can pursue the slave after emancipation. The difference impacts whether the slave can be followed after freedom. Lastly, if the master ransoms the slave for the first crime and then the slave commits another crime, the second crime is treated independently; the master must again choose surrender or ransom, because the first ransom purifies the slave from that liability. This refutes any notion that the master is exempt from a second choice.

Key Terms
جناية (jināyah): a crime causing harm, specifically homicide or injury, whether intentional or accidental.
خطأ (khaṭāʾ): accidental, unintentional act leading to harm.
دفع (dafʿ): handing over the slave to the victim's guardian as recompense for the crime.
فداء (fidāʾ): ransom paid by the master to redeem the slave from liability.
أرش (arsh): compensation money for injury less than life, but here used broadly for any compensation including for life.
مولى (mawlā): master or owner of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave commits an accidental killing, what are the master's options according to the Hanafi school?
A) Either surrender the slave or pay the blood money immediately.
B) Only surrender the slave, no other option.
C) Pay the blood money in installments over three years.
D) The slave is sold and the master is free of liability.

Key Takeaway
For a slave's accidental killing, the Hanafi school holds the master liable to either surrender the slave or pay ransom immediately, and the slave is not pursued after emancipation.

Diyat — Blood Money

Master's Options for Slave's Injury and Shafi'i Difference
States Hanafi rule on ransoming or handing over a slave for injury, and contrasts with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's options when his slave commits an injury, and explain the practical difference with al-Shafi'i's position.

Core Idea
The master may ransom the slave immediately or hand him over; once ransomed, the injury is erased and the victim has no recourse after emancipation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a key fiqh ruling concerning injuries inflicted by slaves. The Hanafi school gives the master a choice between paying the blood money (arsh) immediately, or surrendering the slave to the victim. This ruling is contrasted with the Shafi'i position, leading to a practical difference in whether the victim can pursue the slave after emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
The master may either hand over the slave to the victim (or guardian) to be sold for the arsh, or ransom the slave by paying the arsh immediately. This obligation arises from the slave's act.
The text states 'أو فداه بأرشها حالاً' (or ransoms him for its arsh immediately).
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the slave must be sold for the arsh unless the master pays the arsh. The victim's right attaches primarily to the slave's person.
3
Analyse the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The key divergence emerges after emancipation. According to al-Shafi'i, the victim can still pursue the former slave (now free) for the arsh. In the Hanafi school, the master's payment or handover extinguishes the liability; the victim has no further recourse against the emancipated slave.
The text says: 'وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في اتباع الجاني بعد العتق'.
4
Apply to scenarios of multiple injuries
If the master ransoms the first injury and the slave commits a second, the first is erased and the second is treated as fresh, giving the master full options again. For two simultaneous injuries, the master can hand over the slave to both guardians to divide proportionally, or pay both arsh amounts.
5
Note the disposal rule
If the master disposes of the slave (sale, gift, emancipation, tadbir, umm walad) without knowledge of the injury, he guarantees the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh, because his action makes him liable without deliberate choice of ransom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو فداه بأرشها حالاً، هذا عندنا، وعند الشَّافِعِيِّ يباع فيها إلا أن يقضي المولى الأرش، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في اتباع الجاني بعد العتق، فإنَّ المجني عليه يتَّبِعُ الجاني إذا عتق عند الشَّافِعِي. فإن فداه فجنى، فهي كالأولى، فإنَّهُ إذا فدى طَهُرَ عن الأولى، فصارت الأولى كأن لم تكن، فيجب بالثانية الدفع أو الفداء. فإن جنى جنايتين دفعَهُ بهما إلى وليهما يقسمانِهِ على قدر حقيهما أو فداه بأرشهما. فإن وهبه، أو باعَه، أو أعتقه، أو دبره، أو استولدها ولم يعلم بها، ضَمِنَ الأقل من قيمته ومنَ الأرش.
Or he ransoms him for its arsh immediately – this is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i, he is sold for it unless the master pays the arsh. The fruit of the difference appears in pursuing the criminal after emancipation: the victim pursues the criminal when he is emancipated according to al-Shafi'i. If he ransoms him and he commits [another] injury, it is like the first: when he ransoms, he becomes clear of the first, so the first becomes as if it had not been. So for the second, either handing over or ransom is obligatory. If he commits two injuries, he hands him over to both their guardians, who divide him according to the measure of their rights, or ransoms him for their two arsh. If he gives him as a gift, or sells him, or emancipates him, or makes him mudabbar, or makes her (the slave-woman) an umm walad, and he did not know of the injury, he guarantees the lesser of his value and the arsh.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, presents three rulings within the chapter of diyat (blood money) concerning injuries inflicted by slaves. The first ruling deals with the master's options when his slave commits a jina'yah (injury). According to the Hanafi school, the master may either hand over the slave to the victim (or his guardian) to be sold for the blood money, or ransom the slave by paying the arsh (blood money equivalent) immediately. This is stated as 'our' position, contrasting with Imam al-Shafi'i's view, who holds that the slave must be sold for the arsh unless the master pays the arsh. The practical difference (thamarat al-khilaf) emerges after the slave is emancipated: according to al-Shafi'i, the victim can still pursue the former slave (now free) for the blood money, whereas in the Hanafi school, the master's payment or ransom settles the matter and the victim has no further recourse against the emancipated slave. The second ruling covers the scenario where the master ransoms the first injury, and then the slave commits a second injury. The first injury is considered erased, so the second injury is treated like the first, giving the master again the option to pay or ransom. The third ruling addresses multiple injuries: if the slave commits two injuries, the master may hand him over to both guardians, who will divide the slave's value proportionally, or ransom him by paying both arsh amounts. A subsequent ruling discusses the master's liability if he disposes of the slave (by gift, sale, emancipation, tadbir, or making an umm walad) without knowledge of the injury. In such a case, he guarantees the lesser of the slave's value and the arsh, because his action makes him liable but he had no deliberate choice for ransom.

Key Terms
فداء (fidā'): The compensation paid by the master to free the slave from liability for the injury; it is the ransom amount.
أرش (arsh): The blood money or compensation for injuries not involving loss of life; the amount varies by the severity of the injury.
جناية (jināyah): An unlawful injury inflicted by the slave on another person, whether causing death or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if the master pays the arsh immediately for the slave's first injury, and then the slave commits a second injury?
A) The master must hand over the slave for both injuries combined.
B) The first injury is considered as if it never occurred, and the master again has the option of paying or handing over for the second.
C) The slave is automatically emancipated after the first ransom.
D) The master is liable for the greater of the two arsh amounts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the master a choice between immediate ransom or handing over the slave; once ransomed, the injury is extinguished and the freed slave cannot be pursued.

Slave with Two Injuries and Master's Disposal without Knowledge
Ruling on a slave with two injuries and master's options, plus liability if disposed of without knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's options when his slave commits two injuries and apply the rule of liability when the master disposes of the slave without knowledge of the injuries.

Core Idea
Master may hand over or ransom; disposal without knowledge triggers liability for the lesser of value and arsh.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Book of Diyat: when a slave commits two separate injuries, the master has a choice. But what happens if the master disposes of the slave (e.g., sells or gifts him) without knowing about the injuries? The Hanafi school provides a precise answer based on the principle that ignorance prevents an assumption of choosing ransom. We will unpack the text from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the master's initial options
Read the first part: the master may either hand over the slave to the victims (who divide him proportionally) or ransom him by paying the total arsh. Note that the second injury attaches despite the first, like successive debts.
The division is based on the amount of arsh, not the number of victims.
2
Understand the analogy with successive debts
The commentator explains that the first injury's attachment does not block the second, just as debts on a debtor are successive. This analogy (qiyas) is the proof for the ruling.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on similarity.
3
Analyze the ruling on disposal without knowledge
If the master gifts, sells, frees, makes mudabbar, or makes umm walad without knowing of the injuries, he guarantees the lesser of the slave's value and the total arsh. Why? Because he nullified the victims' right but did not knowingly choose ransom.
Knowledge is key: with knowledge, he is deemed to have chosen ransom (full arsh); without, he pays the lesser.
4
Distinguish between disposals that prevent handing over and those that do not
The text lists examples of non-preventing acts: intercourse without impregnation, marriage, employment, lease, pawn. These do not make the master liable for the lesser because they do not render the slave unavailable.
The principle: any act that disables delivery with knowledge triggers full ransom; without knowledge, only the lesser.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات/ أنواعها وأحكامها
فإن جنى جنايتين دفعه بهما إلى وليهما يقسمانِهِ على قدر حقيهما أو فداه بأرشيهما. فإن وهبه أو باعَه، أو أعتقه، أو دبره، أو استولدها ولم يعلم بها، ضَمِنَ الأقل من قيمته ومنَ الأرش.
قال الشارح: أي فإن جنى العبد جنايتين، فللمولى إما يدفعُهُ بالجنايتين إلى وليهما يقسمانه على قدر حقيهما، أو يفديه بأرشهما؛ لأن تعلق الأولى برقبته لا يمنع تعلق الثانية بهما، كالديون المتلاحقة. ألا ترى أن ملك المولى يمنعُ تعلّق الجناية لحق المجني عليه الأوّل أولى أن لا يمنع ومعنى قوله: على قدر حقيهما على قدر إرش جنايتهما؛ لأنَّ المستحق إنما يستحقه عوضاً عما فات عليه، فلا بد من أن يقسم على قدر العوض. كذا في «الكفاية».
قوله: فإن وهبه... الخ؛ الأصلُ أنه متى أحدث فيه تصرفاً يعجزه عن الدفع عالماً بالجناية يصيرُ مختاراً للفداء وإلا فلا، فمثالُ الأوّل ما ذكره المصنف، ومثال الثاني: وطء الثيب بلا إعلاق؛ لأنه لا ينقص. وكذا التزويج والاستخدام، وكذا الإجارة والرهن على الأظهر؛ لأنَّ الإجارة تنقض بالأعذار، وقيام حق وليّ الجناية فيه عذر، ولتمكن الراهن من قضاء الدين، فلم يعجز، والإذن بالتجارة، وإن ركبه دين؛ لأنَّ الإذن لا يفوت الدفع، ولا ينقص الرقبة، إلا أن لمولى الجناية أن يمنع من قبوله؛ لأنَّ الدِّينَ حقه من جهة المولى، فيلزم المولى قيمته. كذا في رد المحتار).
قوله: ضمن الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش؛ لأنه فوت حقه، فيضمنه، وحقه في أقلهما؛ لأنه ليس له المطالبة بالأكثر، ولا يصير مختاراً للفداء؛ لأنه لا اختيار بدون العلم.
Book of Blood-Money/Injuries: Kinds and their rulings. If [a slave] commits two injuries, [the master] may hand him over to their guardians, and they divide him according to the extent of their rights, or he may ransom him with their two compensations. If he gifts him, sells him, emancipates him, makes him mudabbar, or makes her [the female slave] an umm walad, and did not know of them [the injuries], he is liable for the lesser of his value and the arsh. The commentator said: That is, if a slave commits two injuries, the master has the option either to hand him over for the two injuries to their guardians who divide him according to their rights, or to ransom him with their two arsh, because the attachment of the first [injury] to his neck does not prevent the attachment of the second, like successive debts. Do you not see that the master's ownership prevents the attachment of the injury? The right of the first victim is more deserving that it should not be prevented. The meaning of his statement: 'according to the extent of their rights' means according to the amount of the arsh of their injuries, because the claimant only claims it as compensation for what he has lost, so it must be divided according to the amount of compensation. Thus in al-Kifayah. His statement: 'If he gifts him...' etc.; the principle is that whenever he performs an act of disposal that prevents him from handing over, while knowing of the injury, he becomes choose for ransom, otherwise not. An example of the first is what the author mentioned, and an example of the second: intercourse with a non-virgin without impregnation, because it does not diminish [the slave's value]. Likewise marriage and employment, and likewise leasing and pawning in the apparent view, because lease is rescinded by excuses, and the existence of the guardian's right [in the injury] is an excuse, and the pawner is able to pay the debt, so he is not prevented. Permission for trade even if he incurs debt, because permission does not lose the ability to hand over, nor does it decrease the slave's value, except that the guardian of the injury may prevent his acceptance, because the debt is a right belonging to the master, so the master owes its value. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. His statement: 'he is liable for the lesser of his value and the arsh' because he nullified his right, so he guarantees it, and his right is in the lesser of the two, because he cannot demand the greater, and he does not become choose for ransom, because there is no choice without knowledge.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage from the book of diyat (blood-money) addresses a scenario where a slave commits two separate injuries against two different victims. The master of the slave has two initial options: either hand over the slave to the two victims (or their guardians) so that they may divide the slave proportionally according to their respective arsh (compensation amounts), or the master may choose to ransom the slave by paying the full arsh of both injuries. The commentator (the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya) explains that the attachment of the first injury to the slave's neck (i.e., the liability) does not prevent the second injury from also attaching, similar to how successive debts attach to a debtor. The rationale for dividing the slave according to the amount of arsh is that the victims' right is to compensation for what they lost. The passage then discusses a situation where the master, after the injuries occurred but without knowledge of them, disposes of the slave by means such as gift, sale, emancipation, tadbir (making mudabbar), or istilad (making a female slave an umm walad). In such a case, the master is liable to pay the lesser of the slave's current value and the total arsh of the injuries. The commentator clarifies the underlying principle: if the master performs a disposal that prevents him from handing over the slave, while knowing of the injury, he is deemed to have chosen the option of ransom (and thus must pay the full arsh). However, if he does not know of the injury, he is not considered to have chosen ransom; rather, he guarantees the lesser amount because his right (as the one who nullified the victims' right) is limited to the lesser. Examples of disposals that do not prevent handing over (and thus do not trigger the ruling) include intercourse with a non-virgin without impregnation, marriage, employment, leasing, and pawning in the apparent view. The Hanafi school's authoritative position (mufta bihi) is stated implicitly through the reliance on al-Kifayah and Radd al-Muhtar.

Key Terms
جناية (jināyah): An injurious act that causes harm, such as wounding or killing, for which compensation (arsh or diyah) is due.
أرش (arsh): The fixed compensation for a specific bodily injury, not amounting to full diyah.
فداء (fidā’): The act of ransoming the slave by paying compensation instead of handing him over.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is deferred until the master's death.
استيلاد (istīlād): The act of taking a female slave as a concubine and begetting a child with her, making her an umm walad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master emancipates his slave without knowing that the slave has committed two injuries, what is he liable to pay?
A) The full arsh of both injuries
B) The slave's value at the time of emancipation
C) The lesser of the slave's value and the total arsh
D) Nothing, because emancipation is a charitable act

Key Takeaway
When a slave with multiple injuries is disposed of without the master's knowledge, the master's liability is limited to the lesser of the slave's value and the total compensation—a balanced rule rooted in the principle that ignorance prevents an assumption of choosing ransom.

Master's Knowledge of Injury and Conditional Emancipation: Arsh Liability
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for arsh in conditional emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's arsh liability when he knowingly disposes of an injured slave and apply the analogy to conditional emancipation, while distinguishing the position of Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
A master who disposes with knowledge chooses ransom; conditional emancipation counts as a choice when the condition occurs.

In this passage, al-Hidayah establishes that a master who knows of his slave's injury and performs an act that prevents handing over (such as selling or freeing) must pay the full arsh. The rationale is that such disposal constitutes a deliberate choice of ransom. The author then draws an analogy with a case where the master conditionally emancipates the slave upon the slave committing a crime; when the condition occurs, the master is similarly liable. However, Imam Zufar dissents, arguing that at the time of the conditional statement no injury existed, so no choice was made. We will examine the arguments and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: when the master knows of the injury and disposes (e.g., sells, gifts, or frees), he pays the full arsh. Similarly, if he conditionally frees the slave upon the slave committing an act, and the act occurs, he pays the arsh.
The key is that the disposal (or conditional disposition) prevents handing over, thus indicating a choice of ransom.
2
Identify Imam Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that in the conditional emancipation case, the master cannot be liable because at the time of his statement there was no injury, and after the injury he performed no new act to indicate a choice.
Zufar's objection focuses on the timing of the choice: it must be contemporaneous with knowledge.
3
Follow Zufar's proof
Zufar supports his view by analogy: if a person suspends divorce on a condition and then swears not to divorce, the oath does not break when the condition occurs, because the condition itself is not an act of choice. Similarly, here the master's conditional statement does not become an act of choice after the injury.
This analogy highlights the difference between a binding conditional disposition and a present act of choice.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority responds that the conditional statement itself is a binding choice that takes effect upon the condition's occurrence, just as in the divorce analogy. The master's conditional emancipation is analogous to the husband's conditional divorce: both are acts that, when the condition occurs, produce legal effects that the oath-taker cannot prevent.
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the oath is for prevention, and what is not preventable does not fall under the oath. Thus, the condition is effective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to Zufar, the master would not be liable for arsh in the conditional emancipation case; he would only be liable if he disposed with knowledge after the injury. According to the majority, the master is liable upon the condition's occurrence.
The majority position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is mufta bihi.

Source Text

(كما لو علق عتقهُ بقتل زيد أو رميه أو شجه ففعل): أي قال: إن قتلت زيداً فأنت حر، فقتل، أو قال: إن رميت زيداً فأنت حر، فرمي، أو قال: إن شججت رأسه، فأنت حرّ، فشجّه غرم الأرش؛ لأنه يصير مختاراً للفداء، حيث أعتقه على تقدير وجودِ الجناية، كما لو قال: إذا مرضت فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، فإذا مرض يصير فاراً، وعند زفرَه: لا يصير مختاراً للفداء إذ لا جناية وقت تكلمه، ولا علم بوجودها. قوله: غرم الأرش؛ لأنَّ هذه الأمور تمنعه عن الدفع، فالإقدام عليهما اختيار منه للآخر. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: ولا فائدة في التخيير ... الخ؛ قال أخي جلبي: لأنَّ كلاً من القيمة والأرش جنس واحد، وهو المال، فلا جرم أنّ المختار فيه الأقل، بخلاف التخيير بين دفع عين العبد والأرش، فإنّه مقيد؛ لأنهما جنسان مختلفان، فيجوز أن يتيسر أحدهما دون الآخر. كذا فهم من تقرير الزيلعي. قوله: ولا علم بوجودها؛ وبعد الجناية لم يوجد منه فعل يصير به مختاراً، ألا ترى لو علق الطلاق أو العتاق بالشرط ثمّ حلف أن لا يطلق أو لا يعتق لا يحنث عند وجد الشرط وثبت الطلاق أو العتاق، كذا هذا. ولنا: ما قاله الشارح بخلاف ما أورد؛ لأنَّ غرض الحالف طلاق وعتق يمكنه الامتناع عنه، إذ اليمين لمنع فلا يدخلُ تحت الحلف ما لا يمكنها الامتناع عنه. كذا في «الهداية».
If he knows of them [the injuries], he is liable for the arsh, just as if he suspended his emancipation on the killing of Zayd, or shooting him, or wounding him, and [the slave] did so. Because the master before these acts of disposal was choose between handing over and ransom. And since there no longer remained a subject for handing over without the master's knowledge of the injury, he does not become choose for the arsh, so the value stands in place of the slave. There is no benefit in choosing between the lesser and the greater, so the lesser is obligatory, unlike when he knows, for then he becomes choose for the arsh. (Just as if he suspended his emancipation on the killing of Zayd...): i.e., he said: 'If you kill Zayd, you are free,' and he killed; or 'If you shoot Zayd, you are free,' and he shot; or 'If you wound his head, you are free,' and he wounded him, he is liable for the arsh, because he becomes choose for ransom since he emancipated him on the condition of the occurrence of the injury, just as if he said: 'If you become ill, you are divorced three times,' and then he becomes ill, he becomes a fugitive. According to Zufar: he does not become choose for ransom because there was no injury at the time of his speech, nor knowledge of its existence. His statement: 'he is liable for the arsh' because these things prevent him from handing over, so proceeding with them is a choice on his part for the other [i.e., ransom]. Thus in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'There is no benefit in choosing...' Akhi Celebi said: because both the value and the arsh are of one genus, namely property, so without doubt the chosen is the lesser, unlike the choice between handing over the slave's person and the arsh, for that is restricted, because they are two different genera, so it is possible that one may be easier than the other. Thus understood from the elaboration of al-Zayla'i. His statement: 'nor knowledge of its existence' and after the injury, no act occurred from him by which he becomes choose. Do you not see that if one suspends divorce or emancipation on a condition, then swears that he will not divorce or emancipate, he does not break the oath when the condition occurs and the divorce or emancipation is established? Likewise here. And our response: what the commentator said is contrary to what was presented, because the aim of the oath-taker is a divorce or emancipation that he can refrain from, for the oath is for prevention, so what cannot be refrained from does not fall under the oath. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous lesson, focusing on the scenario where the master knows of the injury before disposing of the slave. The rule is clear: if the master knows of the injury and performs an act that prevents handing over (such as gift, sale, emancipation, etc.), he becomes obligated to pay the full arsh of the injury because he is deemed to have chosen the ransom option. The commentator draws an analogy with a case where the master suspends the slave's emancipation on a condition like 'if you kill Zayd' and the slave then does so. Since the master voluntarily performed the act of emancipation (though conditional), he is considered to have chosen ransom and must pay the arsh. The passage then records an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar disagrees, arguing that at the time of the master's statement there was no injury, so he could not have made a choice. The Hanafi response (from al-Hidayah) is that the condition is attached to a future event, and when the event occurs, the master's earlier act becomes effective. The argument is supported by an analogy with conditional divorce: if a man says to his wife 'if you become ill, you are divorced thrice' and she becomes ill, the divorce takes effect even if he later swears not to divorce. The master's knowledge or lack thereof distinguishes the rulings: without knowledge, only the lesser of value and arsh is due; with knowledge, the full arsh is due because the master's disposal is considered a deliberate choice to ransom.

Key Terms
تعليق العتق (ta‘līq al-‘itq): Suspending emancipation on a future condition; if the condition occurs, the slave becomes free.
فار (fār): Literally 'fugitive'; in this context, a husband who deliberately causes a divorce to occur to avoid his wife's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The master must pay the full arsh (blood-money) for the killing
C) The master is not liable because he did not know of the killing beforehand
D) The slave must be handed over to the victim's family

Key Takeaway
A master who disposes of an injured slave with knowledge or who conditionally emancipates upon a future injury is liable for the full arsh because such disposition constitutes a choice of ransom.
